Chapter Forty-four
In cupping gout and joint pains
If joint pains are caused by cold dampness that has settled in any part of the body, and if these pains occur in the legs, physicians usually call this condition "gout.
If, after treating gout with the various remedies mentioned in this book, the pain persists, then cauterization is to be applied. After evacuation of waste matter around the ankles, many applications are made using a medium-sized bronze cautery instrument.
And if it becomes necessary to cauterize the face of the foot, use a pointed cautery instrument. If the pain ascends to the knees or other joints, cauterize each knee three or four times on each side using the same bronze cautery instrument.
If more cauterizations are required, apply them without pressing the cautery deeply into the skin; it should be done merely to thicken the skin. If the pain ascends to the buttocks or back, use the cautery method mentioned earlier for these areas.
If the pain is only in the hands, cauterize around the wrists in two rows. If the pain persists in the fingers, cauterize at each joint and on the palm of the hand. If the pain ascends after some days to the elbows or shoulders.
So cauterize both sides without neglecting good care and medication. For if one improves their diet and evacuates phlegm, recovery will ensue with this cautery, God willing.
Chapter Fifty-Four
On the Cautery of Hemorrhoids
If hemorrhoids appear in the anus with some intestines and veins protruding to the scrotum, especially if it is a recent onset, do not advise the patient to abstain from food that day. Instead, prescribe gentle laxatives to help expel the hardened feces from his bowels.
Then have the patient lie on his back before you and instruct him to hold himself up until the protruding veins or intestines appear. Then push them back with your finger, and mark beneath the hemorrhoid on the pubic bone using ink, making a semicircular sign with its ends pointing upwards towards the abdomen.
Then heat this cautery instrument to redness.
Until it becomes white-hot and sparks fly off. Then push the intestines or veins back into their place. Have an attendant press his hand on the area to prevent the intestines from protruding again, and place a pillow between the patient's legs. Another attendant should hold down the patient's thighs.
He places his hand on the patient's chest, holding both hands down. Then he lowers the iron onto the same mark, pressing it upright with his hand until he reaches the bone or repeats this step if not reached on the first attempt.
And exert caution to prevent the intestines from protruding while heating, lest they be burned and thus cause either death or severe injury to the patient. Know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work has failed. It is recommended that the irons used for children have a suitable size for their constitution, and likewise for adults.
Then treat the cauterized area three days later with oil to remove any blackened skin, then apply other ointments until it heals. Let the patient lie on their back for forty days until the wound scars.
It is necessary to make the patient's diet mild during their treatment period to prevent the intestines from protruding during evacuation and urination. Then, after forty days of bedrest, use a binder to hold the abdomen for another forty days to reduce excessive fatigue, fullness from food and drink, and severe coughing.
However, if this procedure is followed correctly, God willing, it will result in a complete recovery. I will discuss the treatment of hernias by incision in its appropriate section, God willing. As for hernias that occur elsewhere on the abdomen and are beginning to develop, you can prevent them from increasing by applying a circular cautery mark corresponding to their size. And let the burnt skin be like a third of an inch in width. Then treat it as mentioned earlier, God willing, for this will not cause further enlargement. Some earlier authors have mentioned cauterizing hernias with a triangular iron.
After marking the hernia with ink, draw a line horizontally across the top of the hernia and another line vertically downwards. Then insert your hand into the middle using one cautery iron; the first cauterization is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Six.
On Cauterization of Hemorrhoids.
If pain occurs in any organ due to a fall or blow, and that pain persists for a long time without relief from medication, then cauterization should be performed at the point of injury. The size of the cautery iron used should be appropriate for the size, strength, and weakness of the affected organ.
Should pain and inflammation occur, treat with cupping. If it does not improve after the first cupping session, repeat as necessary because these pains often spread to nearby areas. Therefore, continue cupping until recovery is achieved.
Chapter Forty-Seven
In treating leprosy with cupping.
As for those afflicted with leprosy, they greatly benefit from cupping, especially the type that results from corruption of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cup them, then if the leprosy is in its early stages and has been treated with what was mentioned earlier without improvement or halt, and you fear that corruption may spread throughout his entire constitution, perform five cuppings on his head. One in the middle of the skull, another below it near the forehead at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one from behind on the nape of the neck. Insist on cupping until there is a slight effect on the bone.
And remove its scales so that coarse fumes can be expelled from it. Also cup the spleen in the same manner as mentioned earlier. But if the leprosy has persisted and is clearly visible, do not perform these cuppings on the head. Cup at the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the jawbones, seven on the spine, one large cup on the coccyx at the end of the tailbone, another above it on the same level, and two on the kidneys, one on each hip. Two on each knee, two on the elbows, two on the shoulders, two on the armpits, two on the breasts, and one on each knuckle of his hands and feet. Cup both heels and the palms of his hands.
Avoid the nerve located at the back of the heels to prevent burning them. Cup also on the pubic bone, another on the stomach, and another on the liver. Know that the more you cup, the better and more beneficial it will be.
Know that the patient does not feel pain from cupping as a healthy person would, because his body has become numb. The cups should be of varying sizes according to the size of the limbs and joints, as mentioned earlier in the description of cup shapes.
Then he treated the wound with crushed nard mixed with honey and other medications until it healed, if God wills.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
In cases of numbness of a limb, when a member has been numbed and treated with medications, ointments, and dressings but does not heal, then cut off the dead member according to its size and the importance of the bone, making sure to cut through the thickness of the skin slightly.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals, for some numbness that affects the hand or leg can be relieved by cauterizing the vertebrae at the point where the nerve that moves that member exits.
And let no one attempt this except someone knowledgeable in anatomy and the exits of the moving nerves of the body.
Chapter Forty-Nine.
On the treatment of leprosy.
If leprosy has advanced and no medical remedy has been effective, then cut into it to a depth proportionate to the thickness of the skin until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with ground lentils mixed with rose oil, buckthorn leaf resin, and pigeon or duck blood, each one part combined together and applied to a cloth and left on the spot until it heals.
Chapter Fifty
On the treatment of cancer.
If the cancer is still in its early stages and you want to stop it, cauterize around it using a circular cautery like a ring. Some sages have mentioned that a deep cut should be made in its center, but I do not agree with this as I expect it to ulcerate, which I have seen happen many times.
Therefore, the best method is to cauterize around it using a circular cautery or numerous small cuts.
Chapter Fifty-One
On the treatment of scabies.
If scabies appears on someone and its development is slow, either due to the effectiveness of the remedy used or because the patient is an elderly person with low blood flow, or due to the time it has taken, then to hasten the development of the scabies, make numerous small cuts around it like drops.
Then leave it alone for it hastens ripening. If you wish to cauterize the abscess with a hot iron, heat the one that resembles this shape.
And place it in the middle of the swelling until it penetrates the skin, ensuring that the cauterization is below the level of the swelling to facilitate the discharge of pus. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two
On Cauterizing Ulcers.
Ulcers are corruption that spreads in the flesh, consuming it like fire consumes dry wood. If you find an ulcer in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, prepare many small and large iron instruments according to what is appropriate for that spot where the ulcer is located.
Then cauterize it from all sides until the corruption is completely destroyed and none remains. Leave it for three days, then apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized spots so that all scales are removed and all corruption disappears.
Then treat it with ointment that promotes flesh growth. If after three days you find that the flesh grows healthy without corruption, then leave it alone; otherwise, repeat the cauterization on any remaining corrupted spots and treat the ulcer with a caustic drug.
For it acts as a substitute for fire, bringing quicker relief. The treatment of ulcers with strong drugs is mentioned in the division on medications; take it from there whenever you need it.
Chapter Fifty-Three
On Cauterizing Both Upright and Reversed Pimples.
This condition often occurs at the bottoms of feet, causing roughness that troubles people. Cauterization can be done in two ways: either by fire or by strong water.
As for cauterization by fire, heat the spoon-shaped iron instrument resembling a chicken's comb, made to fit around the pimple on all sides and with a thin edge. Then place it heated over the pimple and rotate your hand around it until the instrument reaches the depth of the pimple.
Leave it for three days until pus begins to discharge, then apply a compress of fresh breadcrumbs mixed with salt and leave it overnight. This will cause the pimple to fall off at its base, then treat the wound with flesh-growing ointment until it heals. If the pimples are not reversed, proceed as follows:
Often, eruptions occur on the surface of the body, particularly in the hands and feet. It is advisable to take a tube of copper or iron or eagle feathers and insert it onto the pimple or mole, then pour into the tube a small amount of acid water.
And hold your hand while rotating it slightly by pinching your thumb so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the pimple, and the acid water may reach its source. The patient should endure for a moment the sting of the acid water.
Then leave it, for the pimple will be removed at its roots. Do this one by one until all of them are removed from the body, then treat the areas with ointments that promote flesh growth.
Chapter Forty-Five
In case of dislocated jaw.
If a person suffers from numbness in the jaw due to cold or feverish chill or other causes, it is advisable to warm him four or five times on the spine between each vertebra and once on his chest. And also heat his stomach with an oiled iron, for the dislocation will subside and the cold illness will quickly recover.
Chapter Fifty-Five
In case of boils occurring on the body.
When boils appear on the body due to cold, hard, corrupted materials, it is advisable at their first appearance to cauterize each boil gently with a hot wooden stick or long pepper root or an iron cautery.
And also cauterize the miliary boils at their onset in this manner, for it prevents their spread and eliminates their harmful effect. However, this should be done after the patient has purged himself with a bloodletting.
Chapter Sixty-Five
In case of bleeding occurring during vein cutting.
It often happens that blood flows from an artery that has been cut while attempting to sever it externally, or when incising a tumor or operating on an organ, making its ligation difficult.
If this happens, quickly place your index finger on the mouth of the artery to stop the bleeding. Then take several iron cauteries of various sizes and heat them until they become very hot.
Then take one [vein], either small or large depending on the wound and where the artery burst, and place the clamp over the same vein after removing your finger with the forceps and holding it until the bleeding stops.
If blood flows when you lift your finger from the mouth of the artery and extinguish the clamp, then take another heated clamp from those prepared in the fire and continue doing so one after another until bleeding stops.
And be careful not to burn the vein, and if you cause harm in one place, treat another nearby part of the patient. Know that if blood flows from an artery it cannot be cut, especially if it is large, except by cauterizing it with a hot iron or cutting its branch if it has not been severed. If you cut the branch, its ends will contract and bleeding will stop. Otherwise, tie it with a tight suture or use medications that can stop bleeding and apply pressure using bandages.
But if someone tries to cut it with a bandage or by tying or using burning materials etc., bleeding will not stop except rarely. If one of these methods fails and no doctor or medicine is available, then place the index finger on the mouth of the wound as described earlier.
Hold it tightly until bleeding is confined and flows over the wound and your finger. Do not remove your finger even with cold water, for the blood will harden and coagulate. During this time, observe what cauterization or medication is needed.
Chapter Two
On Fractures, Abdominal Emergencies, Hemoptysis, and Other Surgeries
Al-Khalifi mentioned in the first book all diseases suitable for cauterization with fire and the burned medication, their causes, symptoms, instruments, and forms of clamps. I have arranged these topics from head to foot in chapters.
And I will follow this method in this chapter to facilitate learning for the student
Before beginning, know that this chapter on cauterization is more difficult than what was in the first book.
Therefore, caution in this chapter should be more stringent because the work therein is extensive. Blood loss often occurs, upon which life depends, when opening a vein or cutting into an abscess, tumor, or inflammation, or performing surgery, removing an arrowhead, or cutting for a stone and other similar procedures, all of which involve risk and fear, and many of them result in death.
I warn you against falling into doubt in this profession, for many people come to you with various ailments. Some have become weary of their illness and desire death.
Because of the greatness of his pain and the length of his suffering, the desperation that indicates death is apparent in him. Some of them offer you wealth, hoping for health while their disease is fatal; do not accept anything from such people.
And let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness. Do not proceed with anything of this kind except after certain knowledge that the desired outcome will result, and use foresight in all treatment of your patients, accompanied by warning of what safety entails.
For in this there is help for you to gain praise and glory and remembrance, O son of Adam! May God inspire you with His guidance, and not forbid you from the right path and good fortune, for that is in His hand. None has the right to be worshipped but He.
I have arranged this chapter with sections following what I have mentioned in the book on bones and feet, so that you may find easily whatever you desire, if God wills.
Chapter One.
On treating water that collects in children's heads.
This ailment often occurs in newborn babies when the midwife roughly presses the child's head during birth, or it may occur from an unknown hidden cause.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone but children, and all whom I have seen died quickly because of it. Therefore, I consider leaving it untreated. Indeed, I saw a child whose head was filled with water, and the head swelled daily. Until the child could no longer bear to sit up due to the size of his head and the increase in moisture, until he died.
This moisture is either gathered between the skin and bone or beneath the bone on the peritoneum. If it is between the skin and bone, and the swelling is small, then one should make a single transverse incision in the middle of the forehead about two spans long to allow the moisture to flow out.
If the moisture is more abundant and the swelling greater, then make two intersecting incisions like this:
If the moisture is beneath the bone, indicated by the hairline appearing open on all sides and water flowing inward when pressed with the hand, then make three incisions in the middle of the forehead like this:
After making the incisions, all the moisture will flow out. Then close the incisions with bandages and dressings, and apply oil and wine from above for five days. After that, release the bindings, dress the wound with flaxseed meal and ointments, and do not bind the head too tightly. Feed the patient dry food with little moisture.
Until the part heals, another method of incision is to look where the bone of the swelling appears and where the water collects, for it may be more abundant at the back or front, right or left.
So make the incision where you see the swelling and water accumulation, as much as possible without cutting any vein, for fear of bleeding causing death along with the release of moisture.
Chapter Two.
How to ligate the two arteries behind the ears known as the 'hawsis.'
This procedure is performed when a patient suddenly develops symptoms of severe eye pain or chest discomfort, despite previous medicinal treatments that proved ineffective.
First, shave the patient's head with a razor. Then, wipe the area with a rough cloth to make the arteries visible. Next, have the patient hold their breath while lying down. Locate where the pulse is strongest behind the ears, as the arteries are usually found there.
Mark the location of the arteries with ink. Then, using a sharp lancet, cut each artery perpendicular to the skull, cutting completely through. Alternatively, you may insert the lancet beneath the artery and cut upwards towards the top.
If the vein is severed, blood will flow pulsatingly, continually flowing towards the front of the ear. If the artery is not palpable, one should estimate about three fingers' breadth behind the ear and then incise using a probe until bone is reached.
Send from the blood seven bowls successively to the center. Perhaps more or less may be sent according to the strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries. Then examine the wound, if any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it off.
Lest a hot tumor appear, then suture the wound with a piece of linen thread and dress it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
On the Arteries Located in the Ear
If a person suffers from chronic headache or sudden sharp pains from hot and moist humors in the muscles of the ear, or severe continuous headache, and is treated with various medical remedies without success, we have tried in these diseases to ligate the arteries of the ear or cut them as described.
The procedure for ligating is to shave the hair from the ear of the patient and locate the visible artery in the temple, as its pulse can be felt easily by most people.
If the artery is not palpable due to extreme cold, the patient should tie his collar tightly with his garment, then wipe or moisten the area with a hot cloth until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the probe which looks like this. Then gently incise the skin with it until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook and pull it upwards until it emerges from the skin, freeing it from any surrounding fasciae on all sides. If the artery is thin, twirl it gently with the hook. Then cut off a portion of it according to how far apart its ends are and how much it contracts without causing bleeding. For if it is not completely severed, blood will not flow at all. Then collect the blood from six bowls up to three.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong double thread, the thread being either of brass or sinew, so that putrefaction does not reach it before the wound has healed and thus cause bleeding.
Then cut off the excess between the ligatures, which can be done at that moment or later; if you wish, you may cauterize it with a scalpel until it reaches the bone to sever its ends, which serves the same purpose or better as mentioned earlier.
However, if the patient has a fever or an irritable temper, cauterization is not recommended as it aids in dissipating bodily fluids. After tying off the arteries, the wound should be filled with clean cotton and dressed properly; afterwards, dry medicines that promote flesh growth and healing ointments should be applied until recovery, insha'Allah.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your operation, immediately proceed to cut it either by cauterization or filling the wound with a sponge and pressing your hand until the bleeding stops; if nothing helps, insert your finger into the wound to stop the flow of blood.
Then rinse the wound with very cold water to alleviate its intensity and bandage it as necessary. It is easier and simpler to cauterize the veins than to tie off the arteries; therefore, you may use a double-edged scalpel to cauterize them after marking their locations with ink.
Press it firmly until it reaches the bone and severs the vein in two places, so that its ends are separated; this cauterization will not adhere to it at all. This is an illustration of the cautery.
The sharpness of the scalpel-like points should resemble two small horns, but not as sharp as a blade, for if they are too sharp, cold will reach them quickly and not cauterize the flesh properly. Therefore, maintain some bluntness in their edges to retain heat.
If the flesh is cut swiftly, this method is preferable and easier than all others. After cauterization with the scalpel-like points, maintain the thickness of a finger between them.
Chapter Four
In the treatment of persistent hot tears flowing into the eyes
If the tears are continuous, and their flow is from veins on the skull's surface with no benefit from medical treatment using drugs, and you observe that the patient's face has turned pale Then he feels a tickling on his forehead like that of ants, and his eyes are drooping and wet with tears that have eaten away their edges and swollen their eyelids from the intensity of the tears; thus, the first line of treatment resort to this operation, which they call "the surgical cure," that is, ordering the patient to shave the hair on his forehead
Then, incise three parallel cuts on the forehead's length, each cut about two fingers wide; the first in the middle of the forehead parallel to the length of the nose, the second slightly away from the temple muscle, and the third on the other side.
Be careful not to cut the arteries on both sides and keep your hand away from the cheekbones. Leave about three folded fingers between each cut, and have plenty of sponges or dry cloths ready to absorb blood.
Then insert a sharp-pointed forceps with this shape from the cut near the temple to the middle cut, and scrape off all the skin and fascia between them from the bone using this forceps, then do the same from the middle cut to the third cut, and finally remove this forceps. Then insert another instrument called a "scalpel" through the first incision; it is sharp on one side and blunt on the other. Here is its shape:
Make its sharp side face upwards towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone; push it until it reaches the middle cut, and use it to cut all vessels descending from the head to the eyes without cutting through the skin's surface.
Then do the same in the middle cut to the other cut. After bleeding stops moderately, press the cuts with gauze soaked in olive oil or rosewater and oil mixture
To prevent inflammation, remove the bandage on the third day and use plenty of warm water for irrigation. Then treat it with an ointment made of basilicon mixed with rose oil and other ointments used for wounds until it heals, if God wills.
The Fifth Chapter.
For treating eye pain and discharge from the inner part of the head.
If anyone suffers from frequent, severe, burning, persistent eye discharge, causing both eyes to become bloodshot and inflamed with weakened vision, the eyelids reddened and scales falling off them. And if there is also sharp pain within the head and continuous sneezing, know that these symptoms indicate deep-seated vein inflammation. The best treatment for this condition is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And if there is also sharp pain within the head and continuous sneezing, know that these symptoms indicate deep-seated vein inflammation. The best treatment for this condition is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. Or, raise it slightly across and start the incision from the left cheekbone to the right cheekbone, going as far as the bone. Keep your hand away from the moving temple muscles during chewing until the bone is exposed and all the blood is wiped off with a sponge.
Separate the edges of the incision with linen wicks or cotton yarn, then suture from above using ligatures. Soak the ligatures in a mixture of wine and oil to prevent inflammation. When you remove them and see that inflammation has subsided, do not rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with the dry dressing that promotes flesh growth, such as a plaster made of two parts fine wheat flour and four parts chalk. Prepare an ointment from this mixture and use it to promote flesh growth in wounds like these.
The Sixth Chapter.
For treating what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear belongs to one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, like iron or glass; or a plant-like substance, such as chickpeas or kernels; or a liquid thing, like water or vinegar.
Or an animal, like an earwig or other creature that does not normally reside in the ear. If such a thing falls into the ear, expose it to sunlight through the ear. If you see only the stone, apply some oil of vitriol or sesame seed oil to it, then try to remove it by shaking your head or sneezing with condiment powder. To stop the nostrils during sneezing, after placing a band of linen or wool around the ear and stretching it upwards towards the cheekbone, often this remedy is sufficient. If not, try removal with a gentle hook whose shape is as follows.
However, if it does not come out with the hook even gently, then try removing it with a soft, little bent speculum. If this also fails to remove it, then make a tube of copper and introduce its end into the ear canal gently.
And seal around the tube with soft wax to prevent air from escaping except through the tube, then pull it out strongly with hooks. Often this will be sufficient. If not, take some Nabatean bdellium or prepared bdellium used for birds, a small amount, and place it at the end of the tube.
After wrapping it in a tight cloth, then gently insert it into the ear canal after drying the ear from moisture. If none of these methods succeed, hurry to make an incision before inflammation or spasm occurs.
The recipe for the incision: first, let the patient bleed into a vessel until he loses as much blood as he can tolerate. Then seat the patient in front of you, turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the base of the ear at its lowest point.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until it reaches the earbone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Immediately suture the incision and treat it until it heals.
If the object in the ear is one of the swellings that grow and inflate, try to remove it with what has been mentioned. If this does not succeed, take a thin, gentle probe of this shape:
And try to cut that type of ear polyp with this probe, but do so only if you are certain that the polyp is moistened by the ear's vapor such that it becomes many small pieces. Then remove them with a speculum or hook or tweezers.
As mentioned before, it is easy to remove them. As for water that has entered the ear, do not let the patient sneeze initially while his ear is filled with cotton wool and he is lying on that side containing the water. If this method does not succeed:
Then you take many smooth, round stones along the length of the fingers and heat them gently over a fire. You insert one into the patient's ear while he stands on one leg on that side, then strikes it with another stone.
Continue doing this with each stone until all the water comes out. Water can also be removed by taking a feather, inserting one end into the ear and then heating the other end over fire until it chars.
Do this repeatedly until all the water comes out or is sucked out with the tube mentioned earlier regarding stones. As for removing creatures that have entered, look: if they are small like lice, treat them as described in the section on insects.
If its body is large and visible to the touch, try to remove it using tweezers and tongs; this is easier than all other things that lodge in the ear. As for removing worms produced within the ear when treated according to what was mentioned in the chapter on otorrhea without success...
It is not permissible to look into the ear in sunlight. If you see something of a worm, remove it with tweezers or gentle tongs. If nothing appears, take a tube shaped like this.
Take a tube that is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top. Insert its thin end into the ear to the extent that the patient can bear it, then use a strong syringe to suck out as much of the worm as possible. If this does not work, seal around the tube with wax as mentioned earlier for stones.
If it still does not come out with the aforementioned methods, use the ear drops mentioned earlier that have been tried by the ancients for killing worms. You will find this in the chapter on ear drops. This device is used for dripping oils and medications into the ear, and its shape is as follows:
This is what you see: it is made of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. If you wish, make the tip of the tube inside the pipe from solid copper. If you wish, take a reed and wrap cloth tightly around its end.
Then the oil or the medication you desire is applied to the funnel which is placed in the ear, and then the syringe with its wad is inserted from above. It should be squeezed gently with your hand until the oil flows into the interior of the ear canal and is felt.
The patient should be admitted, and let what is poured into the ear be slightly warmed over a fire. Be careful that it not be too cold or hot, as the ear cannot tolerate this.
Chapter Seven
In treating acute deafness of the ear.
Some children are born from their mothers' wombs with their ears unperforated.
Additionally, some people may experience obstruction in the ear canal due to injury or excess tissue growth within it, which may not be visible due to its depth.
Sometimes, the obstruction may extend slightly out of the ear canal and be visible. However, those that are deep within the ear canal and not visible are usually harder to remove. If the obstruction is visible, do not place the patient's ear in direct sunlight. If you look inside (the ear), and if you see an apparent obstruction, open it gently with a fine probe whose shape is like this:.
Its tip should be slightly blunted on one side while the rest of the probe is smooth on both sides to avoid harming the ear. If the obstruction is excess tissue that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it gently with a fine forceps and cut it carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a fine, smooth probe and warm it slightly over fire before inserting it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction while experiencing heaviness in the ear, withdraw the probe gently.
Be careful not to injure the ear canal. Then, insert a wick into the ear according to its width and soak it in Egyptian ointment. Repeat this process several times until you are sure that the wound has healed or use a wick dipped in water mixed with powdered salt.
Use the wick. If the ear becomes inflamed and painful, do not remove the wick but replace it with another one soaked in rose oil to reduce inflammation. Continue treating it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water, apply it to the ear and use other remedies mentioned in the chapter on haemorrhoids to stop the bleeding.
Chapter Eight.
In treating warts appearing on the eyelids.
This type of warts appearing on the eyelids may be wet or dry; if they are wet, hold them with forceps or tweezers and cut their roots with scissors, then apply powdered gum to the site.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a hot bronze spatula and press it firmly after cutting; often it returns if some root remains. If cauterized with fire, the roots will be burnt and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
In treating sudden inflammation of the eyelids.
It often appears in the eyelids as something similar to frost in its severity and hardness.
It is called 'cold' because of the accumulation of thick humidity in both eyelids. If it appears prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves in all directions, it is easy to treat by making a transverse cut on it.
Then gently peel it off from all sides until it detaches. Tie it with tweezers and cut it; if cutting is not possible before the eyelid penetrates, no harm will come to the patient. If the cut is large, close it with stitches and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no harm in leaving it alone; the ointment will force it out and adhere to it. If the 'cold' is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid towards the inner surface, turn the eyelid over and tie the 'cold' with tweezers without making a cut, then peel it off from all sides.
If the eyelid is penetrated by the cut, there is no harm. Then wash the eye with saltwater after removing the 'cold' and treat the site with an adherent agent until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In treating the pterygium appearing on the eyelid.
A pterygium is a fatty tissue that appears in the layers of the upper eyelid, which more commonly affects children. It causes heaviness of the eyes and inflammation during summers, making it difficult for them to look at sunlight. To make tears flow quickly.
Therefore, they are constantly leaning on their faces and sleeping on them. Their eyelids under the eyebrows are wet and swollen, with the veins of the nose prominent. When you press the spot with your fingers, you feel the vein between them. The procedure is to place the patient's head in a cloths.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare from it a wick. Make from this wick a circle equal to the size that surrounds the vein on all sides. Then place it on top and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the vein in the center of the circle.
Then make a transverse incision in the center of that wetness with the sharp scalpel, not larger than an incision for bloodletting. Do not cut too deep until you reach the vein.
In most cases, the vein will protrude from the incision like a white piece of fat. Gently pull it out using a cloth wrapped between your fingers, turning your hand right and left to free it. Beware not to increase the incision lest the scalpel slips and harms the eye.
If the vein does not appear at first, gently increase the incision a little until it becomes visible. Then pull it out as mentioned before. Dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the spot to tie it tightly. Some people use salt instead.
He places it in the bottom of the incision to dissolve any remaining wetness, then dress it until it heals. If inflammation occurs at the site, treat it with musk ointment until it heals.
Chapter Eleven
On Types of Eye Inflammation.
If excess eyelashes grow on the eye outside their natural course and become intertwined, they cause harm to the eye and lead to conditions such as constant tearing, drooping of the eyelids, whitening, and thickening to the point of causing blindness.
Eye inflammation occurs in four forms: either by cauterization with fire or with a strong medicine, as mentioned earlier under the chapter on cautery; or it may be due to laceration and suturing, or by cantharides ointment.
It is necessary that you place the patient's head in your lap, then turn his eyelid with your left hand. If it does not invert, insert a needle with a thread from below the eyelid and pass it through above near the lashes, then pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Then turn it with the ointment, and make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid below the excessive hair using a sharp bistoury from the outer angle to the inner angle. Then pass the thread through and place under the eyelid a small pad of cotton or linen, then stitch up the eyelid with the needle.
Like the shape of a fig leaf, except that it should be according to how much you want to elevate the eyelid, for this differs among people. Some may require cutting away an appropriate amount from the eyelid according to how much it droops.
And among them is one who requires less cutting, according to the drooping of the eyelid. Then make an incision with the bistoury on the two lines you have marked, starting from the outer angle to the inner angle, and each incision should be close to the natural lashes, like the thickness of a needle.
Then insert the probe into one of the skin angles, then pass it through all of it. Then sew up the edges with a fine wool thread and needle, wipe away any blood, and stick the remaining threads on the eyebrows with some appropriate substance if necessary.
If you wish, you may do this, otherwise it makes no difference. Then leave the stitches and threads for about three to four days before treating it. If you prefer, you may leave the wound without stitching and treat it with something that dries and contracts, for the eyelid will elevate upon closing the wound and sewing is preferable.
This method of pleating was mentioned by the ancients, but it is burdensome for the patient and not recommended. Another method of pleating is to mark on the eyelid a shape like that of a fig leaf as described earlier. Then elevate the eyelid with three probes, either separate or grouped in this manner:
Then trim the remaining eyelid with small scissors like this, cutting moderately:
If you cannot restrain the sneezing and it does not subside, then take a needle with thread attached and insert it into the middle of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger opening and a third near the smaller opening. Gently gather the threads between your fingers, then raise them slowly while lifting your hand moderately.
Raise the skin lifted on it entirely as we have described, then join the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If there is a hot swelling at your cut or incision, soothe that swelling with camphor and similar anesthetic ointments.
The lower eyelid may also have its margins turn inward, so it is better not to use cautery with fire or strong medicine on it.
Cautery with moxa should be performed in this manner: invert the eyelid and make an incision inside as described, then prepare two thin moxa sticks or splints equal in length to the eyelid and narrower than a lancet in width.
Also, cut off their tips from both sides where the threads are attached, then gently bring them together on the remaining eyelid and tie them tightly on both sides and leave it like that for some days; the stretched skin will die, turn black, and decay.
Until it falls off by itself; if it delays, cut it with scissors and treat it until it heals. When the eyelid becomes adherent, raise it gently without damaging the eyelashes. Here is a drawing of the splints made in this shape and size:
Chapter Twelve
On raising ingrown eyebrows with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs causing the problem, take a fine needle and insert into it a thin, smooth silk thread. Tie its ends together with a very gentle knot, making the length of the thread about a cubit (yard).
Then attach another fine thread to it without tying its ends, and the patient should lie down facing the sun so that the work is clearly visible. Then insert the needle with the attached thread into the root of the excess hair.
Insert it into the eyelid with force until it emerges above the natural hair, then pull the thread upwards towards the incision and insert that hair if it is one, two, or three at most.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair comes out with the incision from the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you have pulled the incision but the hair has not come out with it, then pull the incision downwards using the thread that was attached to it until the incision comes out of the upper hole.
Then put the hair back in it and pull them both until they rise up in the upper hole and you see them with the eye, then cut the incision and thread and remove them and leave the eye taut for two or three days until the hair takes its place and flesh grows on it.
If the hair is short, either attach a long strand of natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer later and then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen
In treating strabismus occurring in the upper eyelid of the eye.
This strabismus is called "entropion" and may be either congenital or acquired. The acquired kind usually results from trauma, injury, or disease etc.
The procedure is to cut open the adhesion, separate its edges, and insert a cotton thread between them. Tie it until healed. Avoid using drying agents that cause constriction.
But if you do this, the entropion will return worse than before. Instead, use relaxing agents such as ground fenugreek seeds mixed with water, crushed linseed oil and diacilion ointment mixed with a little olive oil to moisten the cotton thread used for treatment.
The goal of treatment is to prevent the recurrence of entropion in its original form. The procedure mentioned above is one way to repair it, but not necessarily to restore the area to its original condition.
Chapter Fourteen
In treating entropion occurring in the lower eyelid
This string that is made from intestines is what is called the true suture, and it can be natural or accidental. The accidental ones occur due to a wound, cut, ulceration, or similar.
In its application, one takes a needle with double thread and inserts it into the flesh, passing from the left mouth to the right until the thread appears at both ends of the flesh. Then the flesh is pulled upwards with the needle and cut with broad scissors.
If the eyelid returns as intended, otherwise take a probe and place it at the site of the incision. Then turn the eyelid with it and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid so that their ends meet at the corners of the cut, forming an angle. Until when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this:
Which resembles the Greek letter lambda. Then that flesh is removed to the extent that its sharp side is below the eyelid, then the dispersed parts are joined together with two sutures using wool thread, and finally it is treated with the mentioned soothing and wrapping remedies until it heals.
If the suture was accidental due to a cut, stitching, ulceration or similar, it is necessary to make a simple incision without hair at the location mentioned earlier and then separate the lips with twisting as previously described.
The sum of treatment for suture whether from above or below is that the work be done according to what appears feasible regarding its form, as it may vary greatly in appearance. The experienced physician finds a way whatever the situation.
So that the shape returns to its natural form or approaches it, and the disease itself indicates what procedure and instrument are most appropriate in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
Chapter Fifteen: In adhesion of the eyelid to the globe or cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people, where the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye preventing its free movement and hindering its natural functions. In such case, a probe tip should be inserted under the eyelid.
He raises it above or extends it with a hook, then cuts the adhesion with a fine scissor that is not sharp-pointed but slightly curved so as not to cause discomfort to the patient during the procedure. The cutting should be done gently like peeling off the adhesion.
After the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the adhesion is completely healed, you should then apply salt water to wash the eye or instill in it drops of camphor water mixed with water. Then, separate the eyelid from the eye using a cotton wick.
Place a moistened bandage made of egg white above the eye and after three days, apply cupping glasses until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen
In cutting the foreskin and excising flesh from the glans penis.
The foreskin has two kinds: either nerve-like, resembling a solid thin membrane, or non-nervous resembling firm white mucilage that sticks to iron when touched and can be grasped with tongs and cut off.
And do not grasp it with tongs. Both kinds begin from the largest meatus until it becomes slightly thicker, covering the eye and preventing light and eye movement. The procedure for cutting it is to place the patient's head on your lap.
Then open his eyes, lift the eyelid with your hand, grasp the foreskin with a slightly curved tongs and extend it upwards. Take a needle, insert into it a horse or cow hair thread and tie it tightly. Bend one end of the needle slightly and pierce it in the middle of the foreskin.
Pass the needle through it, tie the foreskin with the thread and extend it upwards. Use the hair to scrape off the side of the foreskin near the pupil as if you are peeling it until its end. Then cut off the remaining part at the base of the meatus using a fine scissor or small forceps. Leave the natural flesh of the meatus intact.
So that it does not cause constant tearing of the lachrymal ducts. You can extend the foreskin with tongs alone or with thread as mentioned earlier, then scrape off its edge using a smooth-ended scissor which looks like this:
Take care that the scalpel does not touch the conjunctiva, causing a cut through which the pupil may prolapse. After cutting, powdered salt or Indian indigo should be applied to the eye and left overnight before treating it as necessary until it heals.
If the tumor is non-neural and an needle cannot be inserted or a thread attached, it is often treated with drugs that will heal as described in the section. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
And take a fine, smooth, and blunt scalpel resembling this shape: Gently scrape the tumor from above with the scalpel until it becomes loose and crumbles. If bleeding occurs, pour Indian indigo or red ochre powder into the eye immediately, apply some powdered salt, and leave the eye alone overnight.
Then prepare to perform the operation until all of it is removed, except if you encounter a hot tumor in the eye, leave it alone and treat the hot tumor until it heals. Then resume the operation by scraping until it heals.
If the pterygium causes severe eye pain, tie it with a thread and cut off some of it, but do not cut too deeply to avoid excessive tearing. Then apply red ochre or Indian indigo powder to the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cutting the pterygium and any excess flesh growing in the eye.
Some people's eyes may grow red, overlapping flesh that covers or approaches the pupil or overflows onto the eyelids, sometimes even causing the eyelids to turn outward like a pomegranate flower.
The procedure is for the patient to lie down on their back while you open their eyes and tie up all of that red flesh with appropriate threads.
Or, grasp it with tweezers or forceps and cut the flesh piece by piece until all of it is removed. Be careful not to injure the eye during the operation. You can use either the scalpel described for cutting tumors or small scissors. How to catch the thread as its picture appears in the following chapter.
If after cutting, all the veins are gone, fill the eye with crushed salt or drop into it red rose water and similar medications. Then apply from outside a cloth soaked in egg white to protect against inflammation.
If there remains some veins, bleeding is profuse and you fear inflammation, leave the eye alone and treat it with something to calm inflammation. Then return to operating on it until it heals. Do likewise for excess flesh that appears in the eye of this kind.
But it is not permissible for you to treat any weak eye by cauterization or operation with iron, nor should you put your hand to such work until you see that this member is capable of enduring it.
Chapter Eighteen.
How to catch the thread from the eye.
If there are blood vessels tangled on the eye which prevent sight and weaken it over time, first look to see if the eye containing these vessels is strong and has no other disease besides them.
Then, the lancing should be done by ordering the patient to place their head in your lap. Then tie those vessels with one or two hooks according to your skill. The hooks should be bent like this:
Or two hooks combined into one body like this:
Then cut those vessels gently with fine scissors, wiping away the blood occasionally until you see that all of them are gone from the eye and the bleeding stops. Be careful not to harm the eye with the points of the scissors. Do this work midday facing the sun.
And be careful in your work not to cut anything other than those vessels. When you are finished, drop green or red saffron into the eye to cauterize any remaining vessels. If you cannot complete the cutting at that time, dress the eye with something to relieve its pain.
Leave it for some days until its pain subsides and inflammation is prevented. Then return to operating on it in this same manner until the eye recovers from this cutting.
Leave him [the patient], and let the head be within the student's chamber, and let not a tear remain nor any discharge flow from before the eye. The essence of the procedure is to open the eye with forceps or tweezers held by an apprentice.
Detect [the pulse], and let the head be within the apprentice's chamber, and let not a tear remain nor any discharge flow from before the eye. Your choice is to begin by pulling from above the eye or below it; the former is preferable. Remove two hairs from the upper eyebrow – one at its tip and one at its beginning.
Hold it between the middle finger and the thumb, then attach hooks underneath its sides. Then hold it between the little finger and the ring finger, and gently pull your hand until you see all of the eyelid elevated. Cut from the smaller corner, either with scissors or fine tweezers.
Do not drop the speculum upon its edge, and stretch out all of the eyelid until it rests on the cheekbone. Do not cut the lachrymal duct, for tears and blood will obstruct you.
Then cut with scissors what is beyond the cilia from above and below, leaving no hair attached except at the cannthus. If you can raise it from there with the speculum, it is better; otherwise, it may become inflamed in some people.
And if it was cut, its pain will subside after several days and the swelling will abate. Then resume working on it.
Chapter Nineteen.
On returning the feather to the nose.
The doctors call it 'the feather'. If you treat it with cupping or burn it with strong medications mentioned earlier, but it does not heal, the only remedy is to cut into the swelling when it becomes ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed. If you see decay or blackness in the bone, scrape it with an instrument shaped like this:
And it is called the 'coarse-headed' instrument, made of Indian iron with a round head like a mortar. Engrave it finely either 'Mbard' or 'Esclafej', then place it on the site of bone decay and turn it between your fingers while slightly squeezing your hand until you know that the decay has been scraped off.
He repeated this procedure several times, then he treated the site with dry, astringent medications. If the wound closed and flesh grew over it, and no further flow occurred, he would leave it alone for forty days. During this period, there was no recurrence of the condition, nor did any other incident occur.
Know that he has recovered, otherwise there is no other treatment for him except to replace the nasal pack into its original position in the nostril using this method: exposing the bone again with iron or a caustic medication. Then take a curved hook shaped like this:
The tip of the iron should be shaped like a triangle, and its shaft should be cone-shaped and tapered towards the tip as you see. When he feels the breath coming out of it upon placing his hand over his nose, then you should treat the site with dry, astringent medications as I have instructed. Chapter Twenty.
On the Return of Tears in the Eye.
If the eye produces tears continuously without any damage to vision, it is advisable for the patient to be separated from others and placed in a dark room. Then place a plaster on his forehead without restriction and apply a thin poultice.
Then place on the eye a poultice made from acacia gum, myrrh, honey, and frankincense. Then bind the eye tightly from above with many bandages, the recipe for which is to take many soft cloths, fold each one into four layers, and make many of them.
Then place one bandage on top of another according to the eye's requirement, then secure it with a strong, tight cloth. Then reapply the plaster without restriction and leave the eye bound for one day and night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after one day and night as mentioned before. If the eye has not returned to its original state, repeat the procedure with the bandages, poultices, tightening, and plaster until it does.
Chapter Twenty-One
In cutting the veins
If a vein in the layer of the veins becomes prolapsed and protrudes outside the eyelids causing disfigurement, then it should be cut as follows:
It is to insert a needle into the base of the vein from below upwards, then insert another needle with a double thread into it from the direction of the wound and pass it through, leaving the first needle as it is.
Then cut the loop of the double thread and tie it around the vein near the needle, pulling it gently. Then remove the needle and place wet cotton wool soaked in egg white on the eye until the veins fall off. Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, the veins can be tied from all sides with a thread to increase the swelling of the eye gently. Then use a thin probe that reaches the depth of the eye to remove the egg white fluid and let the eye dry naturally until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two
On treating Cataracts.
This ailment called cataract is nothing but a cloud that forms in the eye resembling water without any flow. The procedure for this is to seat the patient on an upright chair and hold their head with both hands.
Move the patient's head until you see the cloud descend to the bottom of their eye, then stop and do not let go. If light is restored and the patient can see objects as before, it means that the cataract has descended. If it does not descend despite your efforts, know that it is water.
Otherwise, seat the patient in front of you and take a thin probe. Make a small incision above the corneal limbus with it until the aqueous humor is released.
If it does come out, pour warm water mixed with barley flour or hot water boiled with fenugreek and barley into the eye. Then treat with other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three
We have mentioned the types of water in the section and which is suitable for cupping in detail. So take it from there according to the patient's condition. Then seat the patient facing you, close to a light source like sunlight.
Bind the healthy eye and tie it firmly, then lift its eyelid with your left hand if it is the left eye where there is water, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the probe near the pupil with firm pressure on the same side of the eye towards the inner canthus, then push the probe forcefully while turning it with your hand until you feel that it has reached an empty space.
It is necessary that the distance which the probe penetrates should be equal to the distance from the white of the eye to the pupil, which is the black spot at the center of the eye. Then bring the probe upwards to the location where there is water.
Then press it down repeatedly. If the water flows out immediately, then the patient will see clearly and the probe should be left in place for a short time. If the water rises instead, then lower it again without removing the probe.
Once it has settled and does not rise again, gently remove the probe while rotating your hand slightly. Then dissolve a small amount of pure salt in water and wash the eye internally with it, then place a wick soaked in olive oil or wool moistened with water externally over the whole eye.
If the probe does not enter the eye due to its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes, then grind cumin seeds with egg white and apply it. Alternatively, use rose oil and white of egg to soften the eye and bind the healthy eye with it. You should take the probe called "Al-Burdah" which looks like this:.
Pierce only the skin of the temple with it and do not make a deep puncture, but merely make a slight opening for the probe, then insert the probe as mentioned earlier. When you have completed your procedure, prepare for the patient a comfortable, dark room where he can lie down on his back to sleep.
He should refrain from all movements and coughing, and his food should be soft and suitable for him. He should not turn his head to the right or left at all. The bandage should remain in place until the third day, then it should be removed in that dark room and his sight should be tested.
It shows three things, then the bandage is returned on the seventh day. It is not permissible to do this during treatment or after drawing blood from your wrist; rather, it should be avoided before water rises rapidly due to intense looking.
If a hot tumor appears, it is necessary to cover the eye before the seventh day and treat it with something that alleviates the tumor until it subsides. Then, uncover his sight and place on his face a bandage that directs his gaze downward while he remains in that dark house.
He gradually leaves the house and behaves according to his condition. Know that the learner cannot dispense with observation in bloodletting; therefore, he proceeds with caution when performing it. It has reached me that some Iraqis mention a certain device used there for bloodletting which is perforated to allow water to be drawn through.
I have not seen anyone in our land make this device nor have I read about it in the books of the ancients, and it may be something recent. Here are images of various types of bloodletting cups for those unfamiliar with them:
It is made of copper, particularly; its tip has this specific shape: triple-pointed and sharp.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
In treating the flesh growing in the nose.
There are different types of excess flesh that can grow in the nose, including something resembling a polyp with many legs; some are hard, dark-colored tumors; and others are soft and not dark-colored.
If the flesh is soft and not malignant or cancerous, have the patient sit facing the sun and open their nostril. Insert tweezers into that flesh and pull it out, then cut what has been grasped using a sharp, fine scissors on one side.
Continue doing this until all the flesh is removed. If there is any remaining that you cannot cut, scrape it off gently using one of the delicate instruments to ensure none remains. If bleeding occurs or a hot tumor appears,
Treat it appropriately or, if it is one of the malignant tumors, hasten to cauterize until bleeding stops and all flesh is removed. Then rinse the nose with clean water or a drink after cutting.
Know that it has healed. If the moisture did not reach its intended goal, know that there is a bony projection within the hollow bone that the tool could not reach for cutting. In this case, you should take a wick of linen with some thickness.
And tie many knots in it, leaving the space of about a finger or less between each knot. Then the patient should insert one end of the wick gently into his nose, or as much as he can tolerate after shaping it like a probe, and pull on its other end until it reaches the sinus.
And he should bring it out through his throat, for children often perform this action in schools and it is easy for those who desire it. Then gather the two ends of the wick together, the one that comes out through the mouth and the other that remains in the nose.
Then use the flesh knife at the knots in the wick, doing so until you know that the flesh has been cut by the knot of the wick. Then remove the wick and leave a wick dipped in Egyptian ointment in its place for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all remaining flesh, then leave a lead tube in its place until it heals. If further treatment is needed to dry it out, then use that method.
This is an image of the dropper used for administering drops into the nose:
It consists of a small silver or bronze funnel-shaped vessel with an opening and channel like that of a lamp.
And if you wish, you can make the tube closed like a pipe, and the spout of the dropper flat.
It has a handle at its end as shown, so that it can be held when applying oil or any other liquid substances or medicines.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On Nasal Polypi.
A polyp often grows at the tip of the nose, becoming larger and more prominent over time until it becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut off a polyp as soon as it appears and remove all of it. Then cauterize the area with either caustic iron or a burning medicine. If it was not cut off early enough and has become large and hard like a nodule with little sensation, then examine it.
No iron should be applied to him, for it is a cancerous tumor. I have often seen this tumor cut off, only for great pain to return to the patient afterwards. The tumor is soft and not hard or discolored, yet its removal is possible in its entirety. The patient vomited and tore it off without caution or delay. The area was treated with an astringent to dry and contract until it healed.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If their connection becomes separated due to an injury or the like, know that if this separation occurs in one of these membranes, very little can be done except in some people.
If any of these parts is affected by something, examine if the wound is fresh and bleeding. If so, suture the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals. But if the separation has already occurred and both sides are cleanly separated, do not peel off the skin with a scalpel lest it bleed.
Then sew the edges together, tighten them, and apply a mixture of wax and flour. Cover it with a plaster made from fresh butter or other melted ointment and leave it on for two to three days before removing it.
Change the dressing and leave it until the threads fall out on their own. Then treat it with ointment until it heals. The stitching can be done either with needles as described for abdominal suturing, or with thread as explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the removal of cysts appearing on the lips.
Many people have small, hard tumors resembling raisins inside their lips. You should turn the lip over and cut around each tumor, then hook it out with forceps.
Cut around each tumor on all sides, then fill the site with powdered frankenincense to stop the bleeding. Rinse with vinegar and salt, and apply an astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eighth
In removing excessive flesh from the gums
It often happens that excessive flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients called polyp. It should be grasped with forceps or held with tweezers and cut at its base, then allow the bleeding to cease before applying powdered gum as a dressing
Or one of the dried and hardened warts. If the flesh returns after treatment, cut it again and cauterize it; for it will not return after that.
Chapter Twenty-Ninth
In extracting teeth with iron
Hard and unsightly scales may accumulate on the surfaces of teeth, both internally and externally, and between the gums. These scales can become blackened, yellowed, or greenish, causing decay to reach the gums and spoil the teeth
One should sit the patient before oneself, with their head resting on one's lap. Using iron tools, scrape away the hard scales and sandy matter from the visible tooth until nothing remains, likewise for blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other blemishes, until it is cleaned
If the first scraping does not remove all of it, repeat the process on a later day, then again a third time until the desired result is achieved. Know that molars require various types of scrapers with different shapes according to the nature of the work

Chapter Thirty
In extracting teeth
It is necessary to treat the tooth from its pain with all possible means and avoid extraction if at all possible, for it is a noble structure. Only extract it if absolutely necessary
If the patient decides to have it extracted, ensure that you accurately identify the painful tooth For the patient is often deceived by pain, believing it to be in the correct tooth when it is extracted. Yet the pain does not go away until they extract the diseased tooth, as we have seen many times with barbers.
If you find that a painful tooth requires extraction, it is necessary to circumcise around the tooth with a sharp instrument of moderate strength until the gum is detached from all sides. Then move it slightly using your fingers or gentle forceps little by little until it becomes loose.
Then firmly grasp it with the large forceps and place the patient's head between your knees, ensuring it does not move. Pull the tooth straight out without breaking it. If it does not come out, use one of these tools to gently insert it under each side of the tooth and push it until it comes loose.
If the tooth is hollow or decayed, fill that cavity with a bandage and secure it tightly with a fine ribbon to prevent fraying while gripping it with forceps. Then circumcise around the gum meticulously from all sides.
Be careful not to break the tooth and leave some part of it inside, causing more harm than its initial pain. Avoid acting recklessly like inexperienced barbers who attempt difficult operations without following these instructions.
Many times they cause great harm to people, the easiest of which is breaking the tooth and leaving all or some of its roots inside. Or they may extract some of the jawbone, as we have seen many times. After extraction, rinse with water or vinegar mixed with salt. If bleeding occurs at the site, crumble some gum resin and apply it there.
Otherwise, if gum resin does not help, here is the shape of the gentle forceps used initially to move the tooth: long at the ends and short in the handle, thick so it does not bend when gripping the tooth. Here is the shape of the large forceps:
As shown, it has thick handles that do not slip or bend. It should be made of Indian iron or steel with sharp edges at the tips that fit together tightly when closed.
The extremities should be made like those of a mace, also strong and well-controlled.
