Chapter Eighty-Seven.
In expelling the afterbirth.
If the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, you should order the woman to hold her breath, then plug her nostrils with a cloth and compress her mouth and nostrils with your hand. If it comes out this way, otherwise take this amount.
And make a hole in the cover and place in it open flowers of plants like fennel, coriander, thyme, basil, radish, garlic, and costus root; some or all of these drugs. Soak them with water and put them on the fire. Then place one end of a silver tube into the hole of the pot cover, and the other end into the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then plug as mentioned earlier. If the afterbirth comes out quickly; otherwise order the midwife to dip her left hand in oil of storax or ass's saliva.
Then insert it into the vulva and search for the placenta with it. If you find it, grasp it and gently pull it little by little until it comes out. If it is attached deep in the uterus, insert your hand as we have described until you find the placenta. Then gently tug it out little by little with care.
Lest the uterus fall out due to excessive pulling, it is preferable to gently move it towards the sides, left and right, then increase the amount of pull gradually. It will then come out and detach itself. If the mouth of the uterus is closed, we have described its treatment with fumigation and treatment for difficult births and obstructed labors.
If it does not come out despite all that has been described, do not resort to violence in pulling again. Instead, tie what has come out to the mouth of the uterus using a band around the woman's thigh. Then apply the four-ingredient ointment, as it causes decomposition after several days.
And it dissolves and comes out until, when it decomposes, a foul smell rises to the stomach and head causing harm to the woman. Therefore, do not use the suitable poultice.
Therefore, some early practitioners have tried poultices of fenugreek and dried figs, using the apparatus depicted for vaporizing women during menstrual obstruction and retained placenta etc.:
It is made of brass or copper, resembling a funnel. The narrow end is placed in the vulva while the wide end is placed on fire with incense burning on coals. Hold it until the incense finishes and then repeat with more incense.
Chapter Ninety-Seven
In treating an unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with an unperforated anus that is blocked by a thin membrane of peritoneum. The midwife should not pierce this membrane with her finger, but rather use a sharp probe to gently break through it without touching the muscle.
Then place a sponge soaked in honey and oil on it, treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear that it may close again, insert a lead tube into the hole for many days and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
Sadd al-miq'adah may also appear due to the fusion of a wound or tumor. It should be cut open, then treated with what has been mentioned regarding treatment and suturing.
The Eightieth Chapter.
In treating hernias that occur in the lower region.
Hernias that occur in the lower region are a complex and hard growth near the anus from outside or within one of its sides, with one or more hernias present.
If that growth is left untreated, it opens up and discharges a watery, white fluid or thin pus. Some of these hernias may extend into the bladder or rectum, while others may not. The prolapsed ones can be identified by the passage of feces or wind during defecation.
And sometimes worms may emerge from them. Some hernias, if they are in the scrotum and prolapsed into the bladder or urethra, may also prolapse into the groin or perineum. Those prolapsed to the anus can be recognized even without prolapse.
Insert your index finger into the anus and introduce a thin probe made of copper or iron into the hernia if there is no strangulation. If there is strangulation, use a thin probe made of lead or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair with your finger. If you do not feel it at all and nothing comes out of the hole, neither feces nor wind nor worms as mentioned earlier, know that it is not prolapsed; therefore, proceed to treatment.
If the hernia is prolapsed into the bladder or urethra, its diagnosis is indicated by the passage of urine from it and its failure to adhere to drugs. If it has prolapsed into the groin or perineum, its location can be determined by the introduction of the probe there.
If it is not strangulated to the bone, if there is pain on one side, if it constantly discharges pus, and if treatment does not help nor can it be adhered with ointment - all prolapsed ones are like this - then there is no cure for them.
The treatment for it is futile, for those who attempt to perform cupping on it among the ignorant physicians. But as for those that are not severe and not chronic, healing can be hoped for with iron [scalpel], as I shall describe and have experienced.
Then he spreads his legs towards the thighs, inclining them towards his belly. Then insert the copper or lead probe into the navel until you feel it reach the bladder. If the patient feels it near the bladder, do not insert your index finger into the bladder.
If you feel the probe has passed itself openly, without sensing any tissue or flesh between your finger and it, then know certainly that it is perforated. In such a case, do not tire yourself with it, for there will be no healing.
It has been said that some people are cured by it [cupping] in rare cases. One of the treatments hoped to be beneficial is: heat a thin iron on coals according to the width of the navel, wrap it in rags and insert it into the navel until it reaches near the bladder, then repeat this two or three times.
Until you know that all the excess flesh around it, resembling a chicken feather pipe, has been burned. Then treat him by twisting cotton wool soaked in oil until the burned flesh comes out, then treat him with cauterizing ointments.
If not, he will never recover except by other treatments. But if you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the bladder, with a thick layer of flesh or peritoneum between them, and you see the navel is on the surface of the skin, then cut through the skin from the beginning of the navel. You cut through the flesh with the probe still in the navel until you reach where the tip of the probe ended, then remove the probe and it falls out. Then cleanse that excess flesh resembling a chicken feather pipe so that none remains.
Then treat him with cauterizing ointments until he recovers. But if blood overpowers you and hinders your work of cutting that flesh, the best and quickest way is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp drug, for cauterization by fire combines two benefits: burning the excess flesh.
Cut the blood and dry up the dampness, then treat it with a linen bandage moistened in sesame oil or ground sulfur mixed with oil until it secretes. Remove those burned flesh from the secretion. Then treat the wound with adhesive ointments that cause flesh to adhere.
And these are the remedies that cause constriction and drying. If they harden, then it has healed and no more return is required. But if the wound has reached deep into the abdomen and is distant from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the abdomen and search with it.
If you feel the bladder and there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh between it and you, and it is close to the urethra, then nothing should be done except by trial and hope. One of three methods may be used for this purpose.
Or, you may cut it until you reach its bottom so that you can introduce a bandage and treat it from near without cutting too deep lest you sever the muscle surrounding the bladder, causing involuntary discharge of feces. Then treat it as mentioned before; perhaps it will heal.
Or, you may perforate that barrier and pierce it after cutting near the bladder as mentioned before, either with the probe or with another sharp instrument. Then clean all the necrotic flesh in the incision as much as you can.
Then wait for the wound, including the incision and upper mouth, to heal until it closes while keeping the urethra open inside the bladder. This will be easier for the patient, although the urethra may become dilated in this way.
If the probe is inserted into the urethra and enters one side of the bladder towards its surface near the skin, then take a probe like an awl with a sharp tip in this shape:
And thread it with a cord made of five threads or similar, then insert the probe with the thread into the urethra until it reaches its bottom. If it is dilated near the edge of the bladder inside.
Then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the bladder and pulling one end of the thread, gathering both ends together and tying them tightly. Leave it for a day or two; every time the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues between the two ends of the thread break off and fall away. Then treat the wound until it heals.
If the nostril of the patient is not dilated, dilate it as much as possible without going too deep lest you cut the muscle. Then proceed as described until it heals.
A sharp-pointed trocar for piercing nostrils should have its tip very sharp, while the other end is not sharpened to avoid unnecessary cuts as shown.
Chapter Eighty-One.
In the case of a hemorrhagic anal fissure, its cutting and treatment of prolapse.
Fissures may appear in two forms: either they are inside the anus, resembling pomegranate seeds, or like grapes, ranging from small to large with continuous bleeding.
Some fissures are single, others are numerous; some are external while the majority are internal. However, those that occur outside the anus are usually dry and secrete a small amount of yellow fluid or blood intermittently, appearing similar in color to the skin.
To treat internal fissures, instruct the patient to evacuate the bowels and cleanse the area until the fissure appears. Then quickly grasp it with forceps or pinch it between your fingers and cut it at its base.
Remove it using a rough cloth or a piece of coarse linen, or grasp it with your fingers and cut it. Then sprinkle some sharp powder on the wound to serve as cautery.
Then treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the bowels do not evacuate easily, inject the patient with a small amount of warm water to wash out its contents and facilitate evacuation. External fissures are easier to manage.
It is preferable to grasp it with your fingers or use forceps and cut it, then treat it as mentioned until it heals. If cutting with iron is disliked, use a threaded needle instead.
Then pull the hemorrhoid upward, insert the needle into its base from the other side, wrap the threaded ends of the needle underneath it and tighten them. Tie the thread and remove the needle. Repeat this process for all hemorrhoids except one.
To bleed it, then place on the pubic region a cloth soaked in rose or jasmine oil and leave it until it falls. When it falls, treat it with ointments and other remedies until it heals.
As for the treatment of fissures, often they are treated with poultices made from beans and with constipation remedies. If it does not improve after a while, one should scrape it with a razor or pumice stone until it becomes moist and the outer layers that prevent healing fall off.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, scrape it again more harshly until it bleeds well and peels off, then treat it and it will heal.
The Second Eighty-Ninth Chapter
On the treatment of inverted and non-inverted splinters, dry warts, and moles.
A splinter is a round knot resembling the head of a nail in color, appearing on the body, especially at the soles of the feet and toes. It causes pain when walking.
It is necessary to cut around the splinter, grasp it with tweezers or a forceps and remove it from its root. Then treat it. If you wish, cauterize it as mentioned earlier in this chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, thickened, round protrusion on the surface of the body.
Dry warts often appear on children's hands. Their treatment involves stretching thewart and cutting it off or removing it with a silk thread or hair. If you wish, cauterize it with fire or use a strong medicine.
Both inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed using the instrument mentioned earlier for treating moles. As for a mole, it is also a small, thickened, rounded protrusion on the surface of the body that goes deep into the skin. If it gets cold, one feels a sensation similar to that of an ant bite.
Moles are found on the entire body, but more commonly on the hands. Their treatment involves taking a quill from an ostrich or eagle feather and making it into a tube around the mole so that it encircles its edges. Then rotate your hand with the tube to cut around the mole.
And make it penetrate deep into the flesh and dislodge the ant from its source, and if you wish, you may do this with a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The upper end of the tube should be sealed and hollowed out to facilitate grasping and turning it with the fingers. And if you wish, you may cauterize the ant as previously mentioned in the manner of cauterizing warts in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Concerning the various instruments used for administering injections in cases of hemorrhoids, diarrhea, and dropsy.
It may be made from silver, brass, or copper which has been refined or hammered. And various sizes can be made for different users. So that the ones used to treat young children with narrow or constricted anuses should be very fine. A large syringe looks like this:
The upper part of the syringe where the nozzle is attached should be wide and have a lip where the nozzle is attached, as shown. The lower end which enters the anus should be smoothly rounded on one side with two holes drilled into it, and on the other side there should be a single hole.
The width of the hole should be about the thickness of a reed or slightly thicker. The nozzle into which you place the medicine should be made from the bladder of an animal or the skin of a lamb, and its diameter should be a fingerbreadth.
And punch many holes around it, each about the thickness of a finger apart. Then insert into these holes a tightly wound thread made from ten or so strands of wool, and gather the nozzle together like a small bag. After placing the medicine inside it, tie the top of the syringe between the two stones tightly. Then inject it with this, which is a diagram of the nozzle and hole:
And the manner of administering it is to fill the skin or bladder with the warm medicine as much as the patient can tolerate, taking care that it is not too hot or too cold. Then the patient should lie on their back and raise their legs upwards.
And place a pillow or cloth underneath him. Then grease the tip of the syringe with oil, egg white, or barley mucilage and insert it gently into the anus. Then the assistant should take hold of the nozzle with both hands and squeeze it tightly until all the contents of the syringe have emptied into the bowel.
Then the syringe is removed and the patient's legs are propped against the wall. If the medicine urges him to move, let him do so as much as he can. If he spends the entire night like this, it will be most beneficial for his cure. If it was used to treat diarrhea and its urgency hastened his exit, then repeat the injection until he is healed.
If there is haemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, let the syringe be very soft and smooth like those used for children. This is an image of a soft syringe:
Chapter Eighty-Four
In treating wounds.
I have mentioned in the division the treatment of wounds similar to cauterization, and here I mention its treatment by hand and drugs with perfection. So I say that wounds differ according to the thing that causes the wound and according to the place where the injury occurs.
The things by which wounds occur are many, such as a stone cut or sword or knife cut or spear stab or stick or arrow and similar other things numerous.
And as for wounds according to their locations on the body, they are like those that occur on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and similar other organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds particularly because compound ones will be mentioned in the first chapter of this book.
I say that whenever a simple wound occurs on the head and it is not a fracture of bone, then if it is from a stone cut or similar and has only cut the skin but the wound is large and you fear hot swelling for the patient, then immediately tie off the bleeding vessels at the site. Do not delay this, and let him bleed out according to his strength if there is no obstacle to cupping. Especially if the blood coming from the wound is slow, then let the cupping be done on the opposite side of the injury.
And warn the patient against overeating and drinking, especially from solid foods. If there is an obstacle to cupping, then reduce food intake and avoid meat and drinks. And apply a bandage soaked in rose oil alone or with cataplasm to the wound if hot swelling occurs.
If you find the boil to be hot, then apply pressure to the wound if it is still fresh and has not been exposed to air corruption, which I will describe later. But if it has already been exposed, then apply some ointment.
Until the pus is emitted, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and was caused by a sword cut or similar, and its lips do not meet with sutures, then stitch it together as I describe in abdominal surgery suturing.
If the skin has been stripped from the bone and hangs, but only attached at one point, then cut it off. Then treat the wound with medications that promote the growth of firm flesh to replace the skin. Instruct the patient to consume foods that are strengthening, such as brains and testicles.
If putrefaction occurs in the skin and does not adhere to the bone, then cut off all of the corrupted part and discard it. Then treat the wound. If there is an artery or vein bleeding profusely in these wounds and the bleeding has not stopped with medications, then search for the artery.
If he is injured but does not improve, then cauterize it with the cautery and bind it. And if necessary, even if what has been mentioned does not help, then make an incision until bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and flat, then simply treating them with this corruption while they are still bleeding is sufficient.
Before air alters it, the corruption is made by taking one part of frankincense, two parts of gall and three parts of quicklime or unslaked lime. All are ground and sieved together and pressed onto the wound with good pressure until it sticks.
It coagulates with blood, then bind it from above with sutures and leave it unattended until it stops swelling. The drug will not fall off until it heals. Quicklime alone can also be used if frankincense and gall are not available.
This action can also be done with a bandage in many large wounds, provided it is properly applied and tightened, and the wounds are still fresh with blood, as long as they have not been exposed to air, especially in summer.
There is no need to apply any of the mentioned treatments in this case. Instead, one may apply some prepared ointments or a porridge made from barley flour mixed with water and honey until pus comes out, then treat it as usual until it heals.
If the wound involves a simple fracture of the bone, treat it with traction. The treatment for skull fractures is mentioned in the chapter on surgery of the neck; if the wound is on the neck, the treatment is similar to that of a simple head wound.
If the wound has severed a nerve or an artery, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy but to avoid treating it with any caustic substance such as lead and salt because they damage the nerve.
Do not apply anything cold to the wound because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest organ, requires that the treatments used be soothing, such as rose oil or lotion washed with fresh water and soaked in olive oil.
If the wound is large, use suturing or apply pressure with bandages until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and there is pus accumulated at its base, drain it from the lowest point. If an artery is severed and bleeding is noticed, ligate it or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the tonsils and the uvula is exposed, bring together the edges of the skin with sutures on the pharyngeal wall without touching the tonsils. Then shape the uvula into its natural form and leave it to heal.
Then treat it with ointments that have astringent and drying properties until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, explore it. If there are bone fragments, remove them all or as many as possible. If not possible, leave it to heal and allow the wound to slough off naturally before removing the remaining fragments.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we have described. But if bone becomes exposed and it turns black and gangrenous, treat it as stated later in this book under 'Chest Surgery' and 'Surgery between the shoulders.'
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see it has depth, examine it. If air escapes when the patient breathes out, know that it is a fatal wound. But if there is no depth to the wound and it is fresh with blood, do not apply any powder at first, nor try to close it, lest blood accumulate in its depths.
Let it reach the heart and kill the patient. But apply a healing plaster twice daily, or if no plaster is available, place an old cloth in the wound's mouth to absorb its secretions, and let the patient lie on their side with the wound facing down.
If three days or more have passed since the wound occurred, and there has been no fever, faintness, rapid pulse, or shortness of breath in the patient, and you find all other conditions favorable, know that the wound is clean. Treat it with bandaging and other remedies until it heals.
But if you cannot see it and wind continues to escape, know that it has become gangrenous. Treat it according to what is mentioned in its chapter, with the patient lying on their side to drain any collection. If the chest or back is affected, treat it as previously described by suturing.
If it is large or filled with powder if small, if it has affected the bone and fragmented pieces have broken off, search for these fragments in the wound. If they are clean, remove them immediately. If not, leave them until later.
[If gangrene occurs], it facilitates the removal of the fragments. As for other wounds occurring in various parts of the body, their treatment follows the same principle as mentioned.
Chapter Fifty-Eight.
In Abdominal Surgery and Repair of Bowel Protrusion.
A laceration to the abdomen may be large or small, or moderate. A large one may allow bowel or multiple bowels to protrude, making their replacement and suturing difficult.
Similarly, the small bandage may also cause difficulty in another direction; therefore, it is necessary that its return be easier. As for the intestines, if they are not promptly returned to their place upon insertion, they become bloated and rigid, causing difficulty in inserting them. Hence, the medium-sized bandage is preferable, as it does not cause difficulty in returning the intestines like these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is based on four principles: The two general sutures are suitable for suturing the abdomen and other surgeries of the body. The other two special sutures are applicable to most surgeries, but they are more specifically used for suturing the abdomen.
I will describe each one to you separately with explanation, saying that if the wound is small and a part of the intestines has come out, making it difficult to return them, this could be due to either of two reasons: either the bandage is too small, as mentioned earlier, or the intestines were filled with air before they were returned.
If that is the case, it is necessary to warm them by dipping a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water alone, or by cooking chickpeas and lentils until they become soft enough for the intestines to pass through.
Similarly, the drink that causes constipation is beneficial for dissolving air bubbles in the intestines. Once the air has been dissipated, moisten the intestines with water boiled with onions or garlic to facilitate their return with ease.
If returning the intestines proves difficult even after this treatment, then make a small incision in the bandage using the instrument used for castration. The first remedy is preferable to making an incision, but it may be necessary if other methods fail.
The instrument has one curved side and one straight side, with a slightly blunt tip that is not as sharp as a lancet. It resembles a small spoon, as you can see. Once the wound has widened and the intestines have entered, it is necessary to return them in their natural shape.
If possible, the preferred position for this is on his back. If the wound is located in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie down with their legs higher than their head. If it is located in the upper abdomen, they should lie down with their head and chest elevated above their feet.
Similarly, if the wound is on one of the sides of the abdomen, always aim to elevate the side where the surgery is being performed higher than the other side. This is also how major surgeries and intermediate surgeries should be conducted.
And in minor surgeries, place the patient according to what you are capable of, then have a skilled assistant ready with both hands holding the cloth and gathering its edges, revealing only the area needed for suturing at a time.
This is the method of simple suturing: Take an appropriate number of needles according to the size of the wound, then leave about the thickness of a fingertip at the end of the cloth and insert one needle without threading it through the skin's edge. And with the edge of the peritoneum that is underneath the skin from inside, until it exits from that side. Having gathered the edges of the skin and the peritoneum together, making four layers, then pull tight with a double thread around the needle in all directions until the wound's edges meet perfectly.
Then leave about the thickness of a finger again and insert another needle, then tie it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this with as many needles as needed until the entire wound is closed. Let the needles be moderate in thickness.
For thin needles cut through flesh very quickly, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin. Therefore, it is best for them to be moderate in thickness and not too thin nor too thick. Do not insert the needle too close to the edge of the skin.
So as not to cut through the flesh quickly and cause the wound to open before it can heal, nor pull too tightly while suturing so that the wound cannot heal. Then cut the ends of the needles so they do not harm the patient during sleep, and make himpadding from cotton cloth on each side to hold the needle's tips.
And leave it until you know that the wound has healed. This method of suturing with needles is most successful in minor surgeries, as they may only require one or two needles.
Regarding the characteristic of stitching for a layperson, it is to gather all four edges - namely, the skin and abdominal wall - in one go using a needle with a moderately thick thread, taking care not to make it too loose or tight. Then, after passing the needle through these four edges, bring the needle back out from where you began.
The thread should be knotted at the top of the wound so that the stitching resembles the drawstring of a bag, with each stitch being as thick as the little finger and with spaces between stitches equal to the thickness of the little finger. This method is known by all people.
With this stitching, I performed an operation on a man whose abdomen had been cut by a dagger. The wound was over a span long and about two spans of the middle intestine had protruded from it in the middle of his abdomen.
I left the intestine protruding from the wound for twenty-four hours, then reapposed it and sutured the wound. It healed in about fifteen days. I treated him until he recovered fully, and he lived many years afterwards, functioning normally in all his affairs. The doctors had previously deemed that he would never recover.
It is amazing that I did not treat him with an ointment, as I was in a place where there were no medicines. Instead, I applied gauze soaked in boiled cotton twice a day and had the wound cleaned with honey water until it healed.
Regarding one of the special types of suturing, I will mention it exactly as stated by Galen: begin stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside. When you have passed through the skin and the muscle running lengthwise along the entire abdomen...
...leave the edge of the peritoneum in the side where you inserted the needle and pass it out through the other edge from inside to outside. Then, pass the needle back into this same edge from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and pass the needle out through its other edge from inside to outside, also penetrating into the peritoneum until it emerges at the opposite edge.
Then he also began on this side with his eye near the edge that is in the lower abdomen and drew the needle from the skin near it, then inserted it back into that skin and drew the edge of the bladder that is on the other side along with this edge.
And he drew it out from the skin on his side, and did this again and repeated it until the entire surgical suture was completed in this manner. The description of this suturing is that it is like the sewing of furs by furriers.
That is, the bladder should be sutured once on one side with the skin edge and left once on the other side until it empties. And concerning the second type of specialized suturing that Galen also mentioned, this is his exact words.
And that is, to suture it in the manner that some healers suture it, by bringing each part close to its counterpart, with difficulty they join the edge of the bladder to its other edge and the edge of the ureter to its other edge. This is what I have described for you.
It is necessary that the needle be inserted into the nearby edge of the ureter from outside alone and passed through it, leaving the edges of the bladder untouched. Then return the needle and pass it from outside to inside through both edges of the bladder before returning it again.
And pass it from inside out through the other edge of the ureter on the opposite side. This method of suturing is better than common easy suturing, and this is Galen's exact words. Some have said that the abdomen can be closed with two needles. For that, you should insert one thread into them both and begin by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side. Then insert the other needle from the opposite side and pass it out from your side, according to the stitching of the bags, whether it is even or uneven.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the abdomen, its suturing is more difficult than other parts of the abdomen. And as for treating the wound, its rules are like those of other surgeries. If you encounter a fresh wound with blood before it has been exposed to air, replace the bowel and suture it, then apply a poultice of raw meat.
If a wound has been exposed to air, apply some of the ointments that you have prepared until it becomes inflamed and the threads fall off and the peritoneum adheres. Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have the medications, apply cotton wool soaked in boiled water to the wound from its onset and change it twice a day as I instructed until it heals. In most cases, if the wounds are simple, no further treatment is necessary unless you fear that the wound may affect the major organs.
It is advisable to dip soft lint in temperate olive oil or rose-oil and apply it around the armpits and groin areas. If there is pain or inflammation in the abdomen, often this occurs, so treat it with a warm, decoction of black gruel.
Especially if the inflammation has reached the intestines and become a penetrating wound into its cavity, know that what is thick in the intestines is easier to heal while what is thin is harder.
As for the bowel known as 'the Silent One,' it is not possible to heal it by surgery due to its many blood vessels, hardness of tissue, and proximity to nerves. But if a portion of the bowel has prolapsed and is still soft, replace it gently back into position.
If it has been a while and the prolapsed bowel has turned green or black, it is advisable to tie it with a thread above the point where it has turned black to prevent bleeding. Then cut off what is below that point.
And leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the wound outside so that it is easy for you to pull and remove them when the prolapse falls off and the wound becomes inflamed. Concerning wounds in the bowel, if a small tear occurs in it...
...it may be possible for some people to survive, as I have seen a man who was pierced by a spear in his abdomen, with the wound being on the right side of the stomach. The wound became fistulous and started discharging feces and wind. So I treated him, though I did not expect him to recover. I kept bandaging him until he recovered and the wound healed. When I saw that the spot had healed, I feared for the patient that some misfortune might occur in his abdomen. However, no such misfortune occurred at all, and he remained in the best of health. He eats, drinks, has intercourse, enters the bathhouse, and serves him [the patient] without any difficulty. Some experienced practitioners have mentioned that if a wound appears in the intestines, it should not be sewn up on this basis.
Take large-headed ants, then bring the edges of the wound together and place one ant with its mouth open on each edge. When it clamps down and closes its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not dissolve. Then place another ant nearby.
This process is repeated with several ants according to the size of the wound, then the wound is closed and sutured. The ant heads will remain adhering in the intestine until it heals and recovers without causing any harm to the patient at all. It is also possible to suture the intestine using fine thread.
To remove the animal glue that sticks to it, after inserting it into a needle, one end of this thread is taken and pulled out with tweezers. Then a thin, twisted cotton thread is tied at its end, and this thread is inserted into the needle. The intestinal wound is sutured with the glue thread, then returned to the abdominal cavity. This method of suturing with ants and glue is only for those who are greedy or hopeful.
But if the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the delicate intestines, there is no treatment nor cure for it at all.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
On treating Colds and Nasal Bleeding
Know that any wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and does not heal and keeps discharging pus continuously without ceasing, is called generally, regardless of the organ it is in, a nasal bleeding. Nasal bleeding is called...
It is truly a turbid, thick, solid white fluid without pain, like the hollow of bird feathers.
Therefore, some call it a feather, and at times it is moist which constantly secretes pus; sometimes the moisture stops, and this may be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
And I have mentioned in the division of surgeries that every ulcer does not heal nor is flesh regenerated within it, and this happens due to nine reasons; one of them being either a deficiency of blood in the body or its thickness or because there is hard flesh inside and at its edges which prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because the ulcer is very foul and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is corrupt and its nourishing matter is of poor quality, or because the remedy used for it is inappropriate, or due to an outbreak of epidemics in the town, or because of a peculiarity in the town.
Or because the ulcer is very foul and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is corrupt and its nourishing matter is of poor quality, or because the remedy used for it is inappropriate, or due to an outbreak of epidemics in the town, or because of a peculiarity in the town. As was shown in the city of Saragossa where diseases take a long time to ripen and tumors heal slowly by nature, or because there is one large bone or several bones present, and all these causes and their symptoms and treatments with medications have been explained in the division.
Here, I shall now inform you about the treatment of nasal polyps and catarrh that occur by way of manual intervention. Know that nasal polyps can affect all parts of the body, some ending in large veins or arteries, others in nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, to an organ, to the bladder, to a rib from the ribs, to a vertebra of the dorsal spine, or to a joint of the compound joints such as the hand and foot, some extending close to a principal organ.
Therefore, those nasal polyps that are of this nature are among the difficult-to-cure diseases that do not accept treatment and cause difficulty and confusion. But if there are nasal polyps that have not reached these mentioned places and one hopes and aims to treat them.
And if you wish to learn about this, take a probe made of copper or iron. If the polyp passes straight, explore it with that. But if there is a bend in the polyp, then search for it with a thin lead probe, because lead softens its body.
If upon entry it turns towards the ear, and if the mastoid has many orifices which cannot be distinguished by the probe, then inject one of its orifices with water; that water will flow into the other orifices.
Then examine thoroughly whichever side you can to determine if there is bone or nerve, whether the mastoid is deep or shallow, or if it is a single mastoid with many orifices. Note all of this according to your ability and the patient's reaction to pressure.
And similarly for other signs and causes of tumors, when you have established all this with certainty, then proceed to treatment, looking first if it is:
If the mastoid is visible and close, or in a healthy location far from joints, nerves, arteries, veins, or other places mentioned earlier, then incise the mastoid as described above and remove the swelling, corrupt flesh, and excess tissue.
And if there is flesh growing in its mouth or a wart or similar, treat it until it heals. But if the mastoid is deep and straight, do not cut into it too deeply, then clean out all the corrupted flesh.
Then use the linen thread soaked in corrosive medicine, insert it into the bottom of the mastoid that you can reach with the iron probe, do this repeatedly until the corrosive medicine eats away all the remaining corruption in the bottom of the mastoid.
Then apply ointments that promote the growth of healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal, repeat the procedure described above. But if the cause of the mastoid is bone and you have confirmed this, search for it and cut into it as I have instructed, unless prevented by some obstacle.
If bone is exposed and there is some corruption and darkness in it, scrape off the corrupted parts until they are gone, then treat it with what adheres to it until it heals. If it does not heal and continues to discharge pus as before,
Know that you have not yet reached the entirety of removing the corruption; therefore, uncover all that is upon it a second time and investigate its emptiness and purification with utmost effort. Then constrain it, for if it is sound, know that this corruption lies deep within another corruption, so uncover all that is upon it a second time.
Then investigate its emptiness and purification with utmost effort; if the bone was corrupted, it was a small one that you could pull out with mercyful tweezers suitable for that purpose, so pull it out.
If there were many bones, investigate pulling them all out without leaving any behind with utmost effort. If something from the ligaments stands in your way and there is no danger in cutting it, then cut those ligaments and flesh if they are present, and free the bones.
If it was not possible for you to extract the bones at that time, cover the wound with something that will protect it and leave it for a few days until what surrounds those bones from flesh and ligaments rots. Keep the wound clean so that the incision you made during your work does not adhere.
But if you fear that, place on it a cotton wick dipped in powdered sulfur mixed with oil or a cotton wick dipped in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For if those ligaments rot and the bones become loose, their extraction and pulling out will be easy.
If there was one large bone, such as the leg bone or thighbone etc., and only its surface had decayed, scrape it clean until that blackness and corruption disappear. Then bind the wound. But if a large part of it has decayed and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, it must be broken open.
Cut off all of it up to where the corruption ends. Then cauterize until it adheres, and I shall inform you about a patient who had a boil on his leg that I will use as an example for your guidance; this man was elderly.
About thirty years old, he felt pain in his leg from an internal cause that moved within him until matter collected in the leg and a large boil appeared, but there was no external cause. Then time passed with the negligence of physicians until the boil burst open.
Many materials were drawn from it, and its treatment was bungled until the leg became gangrenous and many mouths appeared, each emitting pus. Physicians attempted to treat it for nearly two years, yet none were skilled in surgical techniques.
I saw his leg with material pouring out of those mouths like a river, the man's body had wasted away and his color had turned yellow. So I inserted a probe into one of those mouths until it reached the bone, then I explored all the mouths.
I found that they communicated with each other on all sides of the leg. So I dissected over one of those mouths until I exposed some bone, which was rotten and blackened and putrid and decayed, so that a hole went through to the brain. Then I laid bare what I could see.
And I removed the rotten bone, believing that there was no other decay in it. Then I dressed the wound with adhesive medicine for a long time, but it did not heal. So I reopened the wound again above the first incision.
I found that the decay was connected to the bone, so I laid bare what I could see of it also. Then I dressed it again, but it did not heal or unite. So I kept exposing and cutting off piece after piece of the bone without any improvement until I had cut off about a span of it.
I removed it with its head, then dressed it with medicines which healed quickly and completely. But I had to repeat this procedure many times because of the patient's weakness, his inability to bear pain, and his fear of death, for he was constantly suffering from severe vomiting.
And solid flesh grew in the place of the bone, and his health improved. He regained his strength and could move around without any discomfort in walking, except that there was a prominence where the bone had been removed.
It is not necessary to expose it in this way. Instead, take a bandage and tie it around the projecting end of the bone, then order someone to pull it upwards. Then take another bandage made of coarser wool than the first one.
Then, tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards. Expose the flesh at the place where you want to cut without hurting the flesh with the saw, and put a wooden plank or board under the bone from below to support it.
For if you do this, the saw will not refrain from cutting into the diseased bone. It is preferable that the cut be slightly above the site of decay so that there is no decay in the interior of the bone and none appears on its surface; thus you would have to saw again.
If the bone is decayed and not protruding but connected to itself, with the decay in its center or some part of it, expose the flesh from all sides then place the board underneath and cut from the side where the decay is.
After finishing cutting from one side, cut from the other side and keep it a little away from the decay. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and saw the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the decay is between two joints, there is no cure other than excision. If the decay is in the hand's bone or foot's bone, its treatment is very difficult, but you must scrape off whatever decay appears and excise and clean it as much as possible.
And how shall you proceed when no blood vessel or nerve intervenes? Know that the saws and chisels for cutting these bones are numerous, according to their position, arrangement, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness and hollowness. Therefore, you must prepare for each type of operation the appropriate instrument for that operation.
Know that the operations themselves may indicate to you the type of instrument needed if you have extensive experience and knowledge in this art, for practice makes perfect. He can deduce himself what instruments he needs for each disease, and I will illustrate for you at the end of this chapter various tools.
Make these examples your models, measure them accordingly on others, if God wills. Picture of a saw:.

Chapter Seventy-Eight.
In cutting off limbs and sawing bones:.
If the limbs have putrefied due to an external or internal cause, and you have treated this corruption with medicinal treatment without success, and you see that the corruption is spreading in the member without any restraint:.
Then it is necessary to cut off that member where the corruption has reached in order for the patient to be saved from death or from a great calamity worse than losing the member, and a sign of this is that the member turns black as if it had been burned by fire:.
Or it putrefies after turning black until the corruption spreads to other members and affects the body, so cut it off quickly; likewise, if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of some venomous creatures such as scorpions or snakes or spiders etc:.
Then if the corruption or bite is on the tip of a finger, cut off the finger without delay; likewise, if it has taken hold in the hand, cut off the hand from the elbow without delay lest the corruption spread to the forearm:.
And similarly, if it is taken in the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow joint itself. If decay has progressed and appears to have reached the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder whatsoever, for that means death for the patient. Use other treatments according to one's ability.
And similarly, if corruption has taken hold of the toe, cut off the toe at one of its joints. If it has affected the whole leg, cut off the entire leg. If it has risen to the knee, cut off the thigh at the knee joint.
If corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy for it except to leave it and submit the patient to death. The method of cutting off a member or excising it is to tie a band below the place where you intend to cut and another above it.
And stretch one servant of the lower band downward and another servant of the upper band upward. You strip away the flesh between the bands with a wide scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or excise it, ensuring that strips of linen are placed on all sides.
So that the saw may not cut the correct place and cause additional pain and inflammation for the patient, if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the place or apply some vessel-closing ligatures and then return to your task until you finish.
Then bind the wounded member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will tell you by way of example about a man who had this very symptom in his leg, which I am describing to you: there occurred in his leg blackness with burning like fire.
And that corruption first appeared in his toe until it took hold of the entire leg. So the man himself cut off his leg at the joint because he saw decay spreading in the member despite the severity of pain and burning. And it healed.
So after a long time had passed, this corruption appeared in the same way in his thumb. He came to me and I treated him with various medications that I had prepared for his body, but the corruption did not cease and began to spread to the other fingers.
I refused his request to amputate his hand, hoping for improvement and fearing death upon its removal, as the man's strength was declining. Upon my refusal, he left for his homeland in despair.
He cut off his entire hand and recovered. I relate this story to serve as a reminder for similar cases of this disease, and as evidence to guide action.
Chapter Eighty-Eight
On the Treatment of Hemorrhoids and the Method of Injections with Medicines.
If a tumor occurs in some fleshy member and the swelling persists until its contents collect, then it bursts forth or breaks open entirely, leaving behind an empty cavity as if it were a vessel.
And the skin covering it is like a rag, having been corrupted without much damage affecting bone, nerve, or tendon. Therefore, it deserves to be called 'hemorrhoid,' but not 'ulcer' unless its duration causes corruption in some of these parts.
At this point, it is called a ' ulcer' or 'canker.' Its treatment involves cutting away all of the corrupted skin completely, especially if it has become like a rag and is not adhering to the surrounding area due to its corruption.
If you wish for the skin to adhere and its corruption has not reached that extent, yet there is still some thickness of flesh on it, treat it by injection. If the hemorrhoid is large and its discharge has a foul smell, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is made of equal parts of honey, oil, wax, and senna. Mix them together in a vessel and heat over fire until the medicine thickens like honey. Then take what you need, dilute it with water and honey, inject it into the hemorrhoid, and tie its mouth closed for about two hours.
Then remove it with pressure, doing this repeatedly until the hemorrhoid is cleaned and its foul smell disappears. Alternatively, you may use ash water if Egyptian medicine is not available. Take ashes of grapewood or oak wood and mix them with water.
Then cleanse it and cauterize with it until you are sure that the wound has been washed. If there is no tolerance for Egyptian medicine or ashes water, cauterize with water mixed with a little powdered camphor.
Or cauterize with honey and wine mixed together, for honey has the property of cleansing and washing, while wine adheres to the wound especially if it is of the type that arrests bleeding and dries. Once you have done this repeatedly and are sure that the wound has healed, then cauterize with something that promotes tissue regeneration.
For example, take the ointment from the onion bulb and mix it with rose oil and an astringent drink, or cauterize with some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties have been discussed in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the wound is narrow so that the tip of the syringe cannot fit inside.
Then dilate it slightly with iron or insert a wick dipped in Egyptian ointment or sulfur ointment until it becomes wide enough. If the mouth is also too wide, then suture its lips and leave an opening just large enough to accommodate the tip of the syringe without further increase.
Similarly, if the mouth from which pus is flowing is elevated above, then incise it at a lower point so that the pus flows downwards. For pus when retained in the depth of the wound prevents tissue regeneration.
If you cannot incise the wound downward as desired, then elevate the organ so that pus flows out of it easily according to what is feasible for you. And make sure that no pus remains trapped inside at any time.
As for the ointments applied to the wound from outside and their method of application, take one of the appropriate stimulating ointments and spread it on a piece of linen cloth, making sure that the cloth is large enough to cover the entire wound. Then cut a piece of gauze opposite the mouth of the wound, making it slightly larger than the wound itself. Spread one of the soft ointments on another piece of gauze large enough to cover the opening and place it on top, keeping it away from the mouth of the wound to facilitate pus discharge. Do not remove the large gauze until after.
Several days, and those at the mouth of the ulcer are to be removed at all times in order for accumulated pus to exit and to know how much of what flows from it is abundant or scarce or pure or not.
And likewise, examine the site of the ulcer itself; does its owner feel pain there or not? Is there swelling or no swelling? When you have persisted with treatment like this and observe that the matter issuing from the mouth of the ulcer is becoming scant.
Know then that the ulcer has adhered or nearly adhered; therefore, release it on the following day or the third day. And when you have released the wound, replace the dressing with a new one and renew the ointment application. If thin pus emerges from the ulcer after a long period, do not despair of its healing.
So its healing is near, for among people there are those whose flesh grows slowly in wounds, and others whose flesh grows rapidly. But if thick pus emerges from the ulcer after many days, know that the ulcer has not healed; for delayed union of the ulcer has occurred.
If the condition persists, increase the drying of medications; let them be wet in substance and dry in strength, like the ointment of the nut tree if you have increased its proportion of calycotoma. And nothing is more effective than treating it with the ointment of the nut tree.
And treat it with rose oil and sprinkle on it the moderate, old wine syrup. Then knead it with this and use it as a poultice; or if you do not have the nut tree ointment, you may use honey that has been cooked until it becomes thickened. Or take an equal amount of gum arabic, storax, and calamus, grind them all together, and mix them with honey on fire. Then spread it on a cloth and apply it to the ulcer, or spread the cooked honey that has become thickened upon it; sprinkle the drugs on it, place the cloth upon it, and bind it.
And you may also use long asafoetida, the blue lily bulb, and ground frankincense alone or in combination with cantarion. Grind them together, sieve them, mix them with the honey described above, and use it; but if the ulcer persists and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is some underlying corruption.
Healing for fractures in bones or other solid nerves and ligaments is as previously described.
The Eighty-Ninth Chapter.
Treatment of nail disease, removal of excess flesh from fingertips, and separation of fused fingers.
The horn is excessive flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb, or perhaps other fingers, and if it is neglected, it becomes inflamed and hot.
Or if it decays and becomes offensive to the point of eating away at the root of the nail, perhaps destroying it entirely, and perhaps reaching the bone such that it has a foul odor, leaving the tip of the finger bare and discolored, then even after applying the treatment mentioned in this section, no improvement occurs.
It is necessary to cut away all the excess growth from the nail with iron, then cauterize the wound afterward. Cauterization in this case is very beneficial. And if the bone remains healthy and the nail also remains healthy.
For the angle containing the nail has caused the flesh to retract inward and become attached, so it is necessary to place a fine thread under this nail angle that presses down on the flesh and lift it up, then gently cut away this flesh.
And apply a cauterizing medication to the remaining flesh until it is completely gone, then treat with ointments until it heals. But if the decay has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut away and remove that bone, for it will not heal while there is rotting bone present.
If you find that the decay and corruption have spread to the finger, cut it off at one of its joints as previously described, then treat it until it heals. But if the nail has been struck or bruised and causes severe pain, do not bleed the patient initially.
Then cut the nail with a sharp instrument at an angle from top to bottom, being careful not to reach the flesh underneath. Otherwise you will cause severe pain for the patient and promote excessive growth of flesh in that area. Then treat the area with a medication to relieve pain.
The supernumerary finger that occurs in some people's hands may be entirely fleshy, or it may contain bone in some cases, and nail in others; sometimes it grows from the origin of a joint between two fingers.
If its growth is on the side of a finger, it does not move; but if it grows at the joint of the finger and contains flesh only, cut it off easily by severing it at its base with a broad lancet.
But if it grows from the joint itself, its removal is difficult; as for one that grows on the side of a finger, first cut away the flesh around it in a circular shape towards the bone, then scrape off the bone using one of those chisels suitable for this purpose.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the union of two fingers, this often occurs naturally at birth or due to injury from burns and similar causes; do not cut through such unions.
Until the fingers return to their natural shape, then place between them a strip of linen soaked in rose oil to prevent rapid adhesion and promote separation; or insert a thin sheet of lead between them until healing is complete.
And similarly, if union occurs between some fingers at the hand, cut through this union according to what is feasible and beneficial for restoring the normal shape of the member.
Chapter Ninety.
In treating hernias.
Hernias are tortuous, hard veins filled with black bile that occur mostly in the legs, particularly around the ankles, calves, and thighs. First, use powerful percussion to dislodge the black bile.
Then apply a cupping glass to the patient. The treatment of hernias with iron can be done in two ways: either by cutting and removing the black blood, or by cauterizing the vein and removing it entirely. In case of cutting, first heat the leg with hot water.
Until the thick, turbid blood liquefies, then tie a bandage tightly around the healthy leg from above the knee to below the ankle. Cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide cut.
Then you draw out the black blood with your hand from below the thigh upwards and upwards to downwards until you discharge as much of the blood as is seen sufficient or as the patient can tolerate, then bind it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black bile; evacuation and cupping are then repeated.
When the veins are filled and this harms the patient, then you shave his leg if it is hairy, enter him into a hot bath, and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and perspires or exercises vigorously.
If a bath is not available to heat the limb, then incise the skin opposite the vein longitudinally either at the end of the knee or below it at the heel with a scalpel; then use forceps to open the skin and strip off the vein from all sides until it becomes visible.
Upon its emergence, you will find it red and venous; when freed from the skin, it appears like a cord. Then insert a probe beneath it until, upon raising it out of the skin, you attach it to a smooth, blunt forceps; then make another incision near this one, about the length of three fingers.
Then strip the skin off the vein until it becomes visible, then raise it with the probe as before and attach it to another forceps; make another incision or multiple incisions if needed.
Then draw it out and cut it off at the last incision near the heel, then pull it and draw it out until it emerges from the second incision; then pull it up to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it out from the third incision above all incisions, then cut it off completely.
If it does not yield to pulling and drawing out, then insert a strong double-thread needle under the vein and tie it, then pull it and insert a probe beneath it; twist your hand in all directions until it comes out, being careful that it does not break; if it breaks, it will be difficult for you to draw it out.
A splint should be applied to the patient afterward. After drawing out all of it, apply wool soaked in rose water or oil on the wound sites and treat him until he recovers; if the artery is twisted with branches having turnings.
The wound was not straight as we mentioned. Therefore, it is necessary to cut upon each side of its branches and places of deviation, then suspend it with sutures until all of it is closed. Be careful while cutting on it lest you sever a vein or wound it, for this would make it difficult to close.
Image of the instrument used to cut the uvula:
Image of the blind suture needle:
It should not have a point like other needles nor be sharp at the end so as not to wound veins. It should be thick and smooth in its curvature because if it is thin, it may cut through veins.
Chapter One-Ninety.
On ligating a burst vein.
This vein originates in the legs, especially in hot countries like Hijaz and Arab lands. It also appears in regions other than the legs due to underlying skin inflammation.
The onset of this vein is characterized by severe pain in the leg followed by the appearance of a black spot at the site of the pain, from which the vein then emerges.
It appears like a plant or animal shoot. Once its tip becomes visible, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing between one and two dirhams, tied, and left hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it becomes too long, cut off some of it and wrap the rest without cutting it at its origin until everything has emerged, as cutting it beforehand may cause scarring and inflammation.
Therefore, it should be manipulated and gently pulled until it emerges completely from the body. Sometimes, it may extend up to five or ten spans in length.
I have heard that in one case, it extended to twenty spans. If it breaks during treatment, insert a probe into its hole and pass a long thread through it along with the body until all the material inside it is released, then attempt to heal the site using medications.
Then he treated it with a remedy for tumors, and this vein may have many branches, especially if it appears at the ankle or in the leg itself, causing many mouths to open from each of which a branch emerges. Treat it as mentioned earlier in the section and what preceded it.
Chapter Two Hundred Ninety
In the opening, on the growth that occurs beneath the navel, called 'ox-humor'.
This disease is known in some of our lands as 'ox-humor' because it often affects oxen; it is a small, single worm that arises between the skin and flesh and burrows throughout the body both up and down.
It can be felt moving from member to member until it breaks through the skin at a certain point and emerges, its composition being made up of some corrupt humors like that of worms and reptiles and the habit in the belly.
Its injury is expected to be such that if it burrows into the body and rises up to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it and come out, thereby destroying the eye – this occurs frequently. If you wish to treat and remove it, then this should be done when its movement and appearance can be felt.
It is necessary to tie something above and below it with a good constricting band, then make an incision over it and remove it. If it has burrowed into the flesh and cannot be found, cauterize the area with fire until it is burned. The greatest danger is expected to be its destruction of the eye, as mentioned earlier.
If it is seen to have reached the head near the eye, tie something below it on the forehead with a good constricting band, then make an incision over it and remove it. It is necessary for the patient to undergo purification of his body with laxative drugs that expel corrupt humors and to avoid foods that cause putrefaction.
Chapter Three Hundred Ninety
In the opening, on the disease known as 'leprosy'.
This disease known in our land as 'leprosy' is a pain that appears in some members, then spreads from member to member; I have seen it as I shall describe for you.
I was summoned to a woman who had a swelling in one of the regions of her arm. I uncovered it and found a slight pulsation in the vein of the arm, which after an hour began to move upwards along with the flesh like a worm, ascending towards her shoulder joint as swiftly as mercury.
If the pain moved from one place to another, it left that spot and settled in her shoulder joint. Then, after an hour had passed, it spread throughout her body until it reached her other arm, as she told me, for I witnessed this circulation throughout her entire body.
I marveled at the speed with which the pain transferred from one limb to another, as I had never before seen this disease manifest in such a manner. Indeed, I have only seen groups of people experiencing pain migrating from limb to limb without having witnessed it so clearly in anyone else as I did in this woman.
I did not attribute this to anything other than the fact that the woman was from the desert, having a lean body and prominent veins. Hence, the moving sensation became apparent due to this constitution, which would not be visible in people of luxurious living with moist bodies and concealed veins.
If you wish to treat it when the pain becomes apparent as described, then quickly bind above and below the affected area and cut into it until the trapped wind escapes. Then dress the wound.
However, if you do not observe this manifestation with your own eyes, treat it by causing the body to sneeze and using medications that dispel wind and alleviate flatulence, such as powdered myrrh or senna leaf.
Chapter Fourty-Nine.
On Removing Arrows.
The arrows differ according to their types and the locations on the body where they hit. As for their differences in type, there are large ones and small ones; some have hollow glass heads, while others have solid glass heads. There are also those with three points or four.
And some have tongues while others have splinters. And those that hit certain parts of the body fall into two categories: either they strike vital organs, which are hollow such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, etc. If an arrow strikes any of these organs and the signs of death I have described appear, it is necessary to refrain from removing that arrow; for in most cases, the patient will die. If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not deeply lodged within the organ, remove it and treat the wound. Among the symptoms of a brain injury are severe headache, discharge from the nose, and dizziness if the arrow penetrates the bone and injures the meninges.
If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow is not deeply lodged within the organ, remove it and treat the wound. Among the symptoms of a brain injury are severe headache, discharge from the nose, and dizziness if the arrow penetrates the bone and injures the meninges. Redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, convulsions, loss of consciousness, and possibly vomiting. Blood may be discharged from the nostrils or ears; speech may become impaired or lost; and a white fluid resembling gruel may ooze from the wound, accompanied by a watery flow like that of raw meat.
Therefore, if these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not yet exited. The symptoms of an arrow wound to the heart are that it feels as if it has pierced something solid near the left breast, rather than entering empty space.
And perhaps the arrow will pulse like a heartbeat; black blood may issue from the wound, followed by coldness of the extremities, profuse sweating, and loss of consciousness. Know then that death is certain; the sign of an arrow wound to the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels following the neck become swollen, and the patient's color changes; he breathes loudly and craves cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be close to the ribs, causing severe pain and very rapid breathing.
And the patient sighs and all his ribs move. If the liver is struck, intense pain follows and blood resembling raw liver exits the wound. If the stomach is injured, undigested food may emerge from the wound.
His condition is obvious. If the abdomen is struck and feces, intestinal contents, or ruptured intestines emerge from the wound, there is no hope of recovery or removal of the arrow. If the bladder is pierced, urine will flow.
And something became visible from it externally and the pain intensified on the patient, so know that he is lost. As for other members such as the face, eye, neck, shoulder, upper arm, back, buttock, thigh, leg, and similar members, they are mostly spared.
If the arrow did not hit an artery or vein and was not poisoned, I shall tell you about some things I have seen regarding this matter so that you may benefit from it in your treatment: once, an arrow struck a man in his left eye at the root of his nose.
So I removed it from the other side under his earlobe and he recovered without any harm to his eye. And another arrow had struck a Jew in the corner of his eye below the lower lid, and the arrow had disappeared except for its small tip which was adhering to the sclera.
And it was a large arrow from the compound bow, made of squared iron, smooth without any feathers. The Jew recovered and no harm befell his eye. And I removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian, and it was an Arab arrow.
It was one that had feathers, so I made an incision between his shoulder blades and pushed it down until I removed it from his throat. The Christian recovered and was fine. And another arrow struck a man in his abdomen, and we thought he would die from it.
So after about thirty days or so, without any change in his condition, I made an incision on the arrow and removed it. He recovered with no harm done. And I saw a man struck by an arrow in his back, which adhered to the wound.
After seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his thigh. And I saw a woman struck by an arrow in her abdomen, which adhered to the wound and remained there without causing any harm or affecting her natural functions.
I also saw a man struck by an arrow in his face, which adhered to the wound and remained there without causing much pain. There are many such cases. And I removed an arrow for a soldier from the sultan's guard who was struck in the middle of his nose, slightly inclined towards the right side.
The entire arrowhead had broken off, so I was called to treat the case three days after its occurrence. I found the arrow wound very narrow, so I dilated it with a thin dilator but did not feel it, and he felt pain and discomfort under his right ear from the wound.
I hoped that this pain was from the tip of the arrow, so I applied a dressing with a strong attraction and maturation, hoping that the place would swell and the mark of the arrow would appear. But when I cut him open, nothing happened to indicate that the arrow had reached the place.
So I continued the dressing on him for many days until nothing happened, and the wound healed over while he remained anxious about its extraction until one day he felt the arrow inside his nostril. He informed me of this, so I put a strong corrosive ointment on it for several days.
Until it opened and I probed it, I felt the thin tip of the arrow stuck in the cartilage. Then I increased the opening of the wound with that strong medicine until we saw the tip of the arrow appear clearly before us, and this lasted for about four months.
Then when the wound became wide enough for me to introduce forceps into it, I pulled and moved the arrow with them, but it did not come out. So I continued to treat him and try various instruments until one day I grasped it securely with well-made forceps, as will be shown at the end of this book.
The physicians used to consider that the cartilage of the nose cannot be restored, but I restored it, and the wound healed completely with no harm remaining whatsoever. And I tell you this method of extracting some arrows so that you may use it as a model for what I have not mentioned.
For the parts of this craft and their details are not grasped by description nor comprehended in a book, but only the skilled practitioner can measure the little by the much and infer new works and new tools from what is absent.
If you have understood this craft, then I say that arrows are only extracted from the organs into which they have penetrated in two ways: either by pulling them out from the place where they entered, or by extracting them from the opposite side.
If the arrowhead is prominent in a fleshy location, it should be pulled out and removed. However, if it does not yield at its initial time of impact, then you should leave it alone until the surrounding flesh decays, making it easier to draw out and remove. This applies similarly when embedded within bone.
If it does not yield to removal, also leave it alone for a few days and then attempt daily pulling and moving; if it still does not come out after some time, you should carefully bore around the arrow with a thin borer on all sides within the same bone until the space around the arrow is widened, then pull it out.
If an embedded arrowhead is in the skull and has pierced one of the brain's ventricles, showing some of the symptoms previously mentioned, do not attempt to pull out the arrow and leave it alone until the matter resolves itself after a few days.
In attempting to draw out the arrow and remove it, if it is deeply embedded and does not yield to pulling, use the borer around the arrowhead as described earlier, then treat the wound until it heals. However, if the arrow has disappeared within the body. And if the arrowhead cannot be felt, search for it using tweezers. If you find it, use suitable tools to pull it out. However, if the wound is too narrow and the arrowhead is deeply embedded without touching bone, nerve, or blood vessel.
Make an incision around the wound to widen it and then extract the arrow. If the arrowhead has two barbs, carefully cut away the surrounding flesh from both sides using any means possible. However, if you cannot remove the flesh around the barbs without breaking them off, twist and pull until they are released.
When attempting to extract the arrow from any location, use tweezers to grasp it from all sides until it is freed. Do so gently to avoid breaking the arrowhead and making extraction difficult. If you are unable to remove it within your appointed time.
Leave it alone for a time until the flesh around it decays, then return to it. If bleeding intervenes, use the treatments mentioned earlier in their chapter. Take care not to cut any vein, nerve, or tendon; use cunning instead.
Remove the arrow gently and carefully, following the steps I have described. When pulling out the arrow, ensure the patient's position is the same as it was when the arrow struck him; this will be most advantageous.
If this is not possible, use whatever position you can. As for an arrow exiting from the opposite side, it may have a protruding tip or you may feel its point pressing against the skin nearby and visible.
Make an incision over it, the size of which should accommodate the forceps. Then pull it out; if it sticks in the bone, rotate your hand until the arrow affects the bone and creates more space for itself, then pull it out.
If it will not come out, leave it alone until it emerges on its own. If the arrow's shaft is still present, use it to push it through; if the shaft has fallen off and you wish to use a pusher, insert the hollow instrument into the tail of the arrow and use it to push it through.
If the arrow is hollow, use an instrument that fits into its cavity to push it through. If the arrowhead is poisoned, it is necessary to excise all the affected flesh if possible, then treat it with an appropriate remedy.
If the arrow struck the chest, abdomen, bladder or flank and is close to any internal organ that can be felt with the palpator, make an incision over it without cutting any vein or nerve, then remove it.
Then suture the wound if necessary and treat it until it heals. Image of the forceps used to extract arrows:
Its tips resemble the beak of a bird, crafted in such a way that when it grips an arrow or anything else, it does not let go. They come in various sizes: large, small, and medium, each suited to the size of the arrow, wound width, and narrowness.

Chapter Fifty-Nine
In the matter of bloodletting
The thirty veins that it is customary to bleed in the body, six of which are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the temporals, and the two arteries in the temples; and the two veins in the corners of the eyes known as the lookers.
And the vein standing in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two veins in the neck called the jugulars, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth and the two veins in the lower lip; these veins are known as the circumoral veins. And the two veins under the tongue.
As for the five veins that are bled in the arm and hand, one of them is the cephalic vein which is on the lateral side and is commonly called the head vein; the middle cubital vein which is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic vein and is commonly called the body vein.
And the basilica vein which is on the medial side and is also called the axillary vein; it is commonly called the abdominal vein. And the radial vein which lies on the forearm and is the one that adheres to it, it is also the one that appears distinctly above the thumb. And the ulnar vein.
And the vein between the little finger and ring finger has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins: one below the patella on the lateral side, the second is the saphenous vein located at the heel on the medial side, and the third is the sural nerve located at the ankle on the lateral side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them. As for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit is in bleeding them for chronic colds and headaches and fever and chronic bad head sores, and how to bleed them as I will describe.
It is to shave the head of the patient and rub it with a coarse cloth behind the ears where the veins are, rubbing well. Then choke the patient's neck with a scarf until the veins appear and their location behind the ears in the lower part of the head is felt with your finger, and where you feel the pulsation under your finger there make a mark with ink.
Then take a sharp lancet, known as the Nisha, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the lancet reaches the bone.
