Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein along with the skin in a straight line, the length of which should be approximately that of two folded fingers.
Or similarly, draw out as much blood as desired, then tie them with bandages and leave them until they heal. The two arteries on the temples are beneficial for treating chronic migraines, stubborn headaches, persistent eye inflammation, and sharp, constant discharge from the eyes.
To perform this procedure, the patient should tie a cloth around their head until the temples' arteries become clearly visible and palpable. Then, using a pen, mark the point above the upper temple artery with your index finger.
Then insert the Nisha lancet from below and raise the artery upwards, cutting it as you did with the other veins. Draw out as much blood as needed, then release the patient's bandage and press your finger on the artery for a moment.
Then place a bandage and gauze on it, tie it tightly from above, and leave it until it heals. The forehead vein is beneficial for treating chronic facial conditions such as measles, sores, and persistent freckles when used in conjunction with cupping therapy.
To perform this procedure, as described, the patient should tie a cloth around their head until the vein becomes visible. Then take the instrument called the "pickaxe," which looks like this:
Place the sharp point of the pickaxe on the vein and strike it from above with a comb or similar object, allowing blood to flow until the desired amount is reached. Then release the patient's headband and tie it tightly until healed.
It may also be opened with a broad-bladed lancet, but it is not advisable for the blade to be sharp-pointed like other lancets, as it should be slightly blunt and rounded at the tip to prevent accidentally breaking the bone nearby.
As for the veins on either side of the eye socket, they are beneficial for treating itching in the eyes, redness, inflammation, and facial diseases. The method to cauterize them is: have the patient tie a bandage around their neck, then sit facing their head while performing the procedure. Make a slight diagonal cut using a small wide-bladed knife.
If the spot has no flesh, then if the knife's tip is thin it may break. Send blood from a vein and apply gauze to them, tying them for one night before removing them. Cauterizing the veins of the nose treats acute fever.
For severe headache and facial diseases such as chronic inflamed pimples on the nose, cauterization is beneficial. The method: Have the patient tie a bandage around their neck, then hold their nose with your left hand while taking a long thin knife.
And pierce it in the middle of the nostril itself, between the cheeks of the nose because the vein does not appear there by sight. Then press your hand on the knife a little and send blood from that vein. Tie it for one night as it quickly heals.
And the veins on the inner thighs are beneficial for cauterization in cases of shortness of breath, leprosy, and dark diseases that appear on the skin's surface such as black spots, moles, and bad ulcers. The method: Have the patient tie a bandage under them around their neck while standing at their head.
The patient sits on a chair, then cauterize the vein diagonally in a wide cut. Send out an appropriate amount of blood or according to what is needed, then do the same for the other vein and untie the bandage.
And tie the veins with a moderate force so the patient does not suffocate. Leave them overnight as the wound will heal by tomorrow. And the veins on the ankles: cauterizing them after treating the crown of the head treats bad breath, gum disease, and bad ulcers.
Those occurring in the nose and around it, and how to cauterize them: Have the patient sit before you, tie their neck with a scarf, then turn their cheeks and observe the two veins; one is seen on the right side of the lip, the other on its left. Recognize them by their darkness.
And around them are small, dark veins; cut these off completely. If you have difficulty identifying which ones they are, target the largest and most apparent one, and do likewise with those in the lower lip.
And as for the two veins beneath the tongue, it is beneficial to cauterize them after treating the abscess of the tonsils for throat infections and mouth diseases. To do this, seat the patient before you facing the sun, raise their tongue, and observe the vein on one side of the underside of the tongue and another on its opposite side; recognize them by their darkness.
So cauterize them, but be cautious not to overdo cutting them, for beneath them are arteries; there might be bleeding from those arteries. As for the three veins that are cauterized in the elbow, it is customary for everyone to have them cauterized, and its cautery is performed in two ways.
Either by branding with a wide, curved or olive-shaped iron cautery applied gently, or by cutting with a scalpel-like instrument called the 'Nashal' [this is its shape]: Shape of the wide, curved rue cautery:
It is wide as you see, suitable for opening the rounded veins that are filled and protruding, visible, hard, and contain thick, dark blood. This is the shape of the olive-shaped cautery:
This cautery is less wide and has sharper edges, suitable for cauterizing the small veins that contain thin, yellowish blood. This is the shape of the 'Nashal' cautery:
This 'Nashal', which is suitable for cutting, comes in various widths and thinnesses according to the size of the veins, also their narrowness. They can be distinguished from one another, and they are well-known among craftsmen.
And of the basilic vein, which is one of these three vessels, its benefit in cutting it is that it attracts blood from the diseases that are under the neck and throat towards the chest and abdomen. And it is necessary for the cutter to be cautious upon its cutting, as there is an artery beneath it; so if he errs and presses too deeply with the lancet, he will cut that artery.
It is therefore necessary that it not be cut with the lancet, but rather cut open with a knife. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then one should avoid it and turn to another vessel or search for some of its branches.
Or else, one can cut the brachial artery in its place as it is clear. Cut it with the pointed lancet as mentioned previously. But if you want to cut it specifically, then before tying the arm, examine the site until you recognize the pulse point and mark it with ink.
Then tie the arm and cut the vein with a pointed lancet as mentioned earlier, taking care to strike away from the artery. And if you see swelling at the place where you marked with ink when tying the bandage, know that it is the distention of the artery, so avoid it.
If you see that the blood gushes forth like urine, and it is thin and red, then know that it is arterial blood. In this case, promptly place your finger upon it for a long time, then remove your finger; if the bleeding ceases, often it will stop. Then tie the arm.
And leave it, warning the patient not to neglect it. Let him keep his arm on a pillow and not move it until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and you are unable to control it with medication, then cauterize the artery if it appears to you; its ends will shrink and the bleeding will cease.
Or take an onion skin, cut it open and take one half of it. Tie it firmly over the vein with a bandage, leaving it on overnight. If the bleeding stops, well and good; otherwise treat it as mentioned previously.
Know that when the wound is small and the artery in the arm is easily ligated, then it is beneficial to tie off the dark vein [i.e., the saphenous vein], as it draws blood from the top of the head and the lower extremities.
It is preferable for the one tying [the vein] to be on the neck of the tie, as there is a nerve beneath it. If the incision is made too deep and the nerve is injured, numbness occurs which is difficult to treat, and perhaps will not heal at all.
In some people, [the nerve] is hidden and thin, hardly noticeable. In this case, the ligation should be made by a nick with a scalpel, avoiding the nerve altogether. If the vein lies between two nerves, cut it lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, it is beneficial to ligate it as it draws blood from the head.
Among the diseases of the eyes, it is particularly beneficial to ligate this vein [i.e., the great saphenous vein], especially if you wish to do so; use the olive-handled scalpel or the wide, rose-hilted scalpel for this purpose, as it is safer than all other veins because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
But when ligating, avoid cutting the muscle head with the scalpel and seek the soft spot instead. It is not harmful if the first strike does not hit; repeat the ligation as many times as necessary, although some people may swell if not ligated on the first attempt.
But swelling is not harmful. As for the method of ligation, its complications and what should precede its repair, know that ligation is either used solely to preserve health.
Or it is used in diseases, and what is used for preserving health and maintaining it, as well as preventing the occurrence of diseases, is ligating one of the three veins in the elbow: the cephalic, the dark (basilic), or the great saphenous vein. This ligation should be performed at the beginning of spring if signs of plethora are apparent.
And ligation should be performed on a Sunday or Tuesday after noon has passed three hours. As for ligations used to treat diseases, there is no specific time limit, but they should be done whenever necessary, day or night.
At every hour and at all times, it is not befitting to perform bloodletting on children until they reach fourteen years of age nor on the elderly who have exceeded sixty years. When one intends to perform bloodletting for any reason.
It is necessary to clean his bowel before bloodletting with a gentle enema if it contains much retained waste, lest putrid dregs be drawn into the veins during bloodletting causing harm to the vital organs. Bloodletting should not be performed on the sick or intoxicated until that state has passed.
And bloodletting should also be avoided after childbirth, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, exertion, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and anything else that weakens the body or the soul. Then, examine the thinning of the humors before that if the blood is thick.
With foods, drinks, and drugs if that is possible. Then enter the bathhouse if nothing prevents it or engage in some exercise so that the blood becomes fluid and his bloodletting takes place in the morning as mentioned earlier, aiming to empty his chest of all negative psychological states that day.
Like sorrow, anger, fear, and all negative bodily conditions such as excessive exertion and sexual intercourse. And prepare his surroundings with things commonly used by people according to one's ability, like fragrant substances, perfumes, incense, and the like.
Then the bloodletter sits on a cushion higher than the one on which the patient sits. He then brings out his arm, and the bloodletter rubs it twice or thrice with his hand, then ties the bandage tightly and twists it twice or thrice, ensuring that the tension is moderate.
For excessive tightening either prevents the blood flow or if too loose also prevents it. After tying, the patient should rub his hands together to stimulate warmth.
Until the veins become swollen and distinct to touch. Then the bloodletter wipes the lancet with a little old oil, especially, and places his left hand's index finger on the same vein beneath the point where he intends to make the incision, slightly pressing down so that the vein does not escape the blow. Some veins are like strings that retreat during phlebotomy, while others are filled with wind so that when a lancet is placed upon them, they sink beneath it and deceive the one performing the procedure, not opening until much pressure has been applied.
It is therefore necessary that the phlebotomist be careful and deliberate in all these matters, then let him apply the lancet. If it opens the vein with ease, well and good; otherwise, he should repeat the procedure slightly lower or higher than the initial location with haste, provided that the site does not become swollen.
If the patient swells or becomes anxious, leave him for a day or two and do not apply a bandage, as it might bring heat to the area. Do not make him enter the bathhouse, then resume phlebotomy if desired. If the lancet was inserted and the opening was small, with thin blood flowing.
I feared that not enough blood would flow, so I inserted the lancet into the same hole again, gently and in a straight line, increasing the opening slightly. Do this quickly before the site becomes swollen, as many people's sites swell even with a small opening.
If it has become swollen, do not repeat the procedure upon it again, for it will be of no use. Place upon it some powdered incense mixed with oil, as this facilitates blood flow and is better in this regard than plain oil or any other fat.
So use powdered incense mixed with oil in all phlebotomies when blood flow is difficult. Also, the drugs Faroukh Triage and Schizandra can be used on the site, as they cause thick blood to become thin and fluid.
If a large swelling occurs at the phlebotomy site, especially in those who have not undergone venesection before or had a small vein opening, quickly apply a warm compress of saltwater-soaked sponge to it and leave it there for some time, as it dissolves the swelling.
It is preferable to do this after blood has completely ceased flowing from the vein or from another vein. If there remains any blackness or greenness at the site after a few days, it causes no harm. If desired, apply a solution of myrrh and frankincense to it.
And some of the juice of fennel and the like, for often a swelling and tumor appears at the vein when bloodletting is done. Place your hand upon it; if it yields to gentle pressure when you press upon it, that is a bad tumor. Therefore, beware of applying anything we have mentioned to it.
For it may be that the blood of an artery has flowed out from it, so bind it with something that constrains and stops the flow. Then treat him with other remedies until he recovers. It is proper to let out blood in proportion to one's strength and the prevailing color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it becomes red. And if it appears thick, thin it out until it thins. And if it is sharp, remove its sharpness. This applies particularly to those who are full-bodied and strong and require a sudden release of blood.
That bloodletting should be done in wide veins and the lancet should be thick. For the weak, it is the opposite of this. It is preferable to do it repeatedly with a narrow opening. The best instrument for bloodletting from veins is a sharp, pointed, slit blade without a guard.
This method of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and cutting nerves. It is gentler and safer than bloodletting across or lengthwise. For those who are prone to fainting during bloodletting, it is preferable to feed them before the procedure with bread soaked in pomegranate juice or tamarind water.
If the lancet is sharp and blood has been drawn three or four times, especially if the patient has a cold temperament, let him take bread soaked in syrup of figs, or honey syrup perfumed with ambergris, or good rosewater syrup before bloodletting.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting due to excessive bleeding, let him drink beef broth and thin rosewater syrup. Use frankincense for perfuming and apply it to his chest. Also use the other remedies mentioned in the chapter on fainting.
That which is from sneezing. And as for one who wishes to loosen his arm and release his blood a second time, if his bloodletting was for much evacuation and his strength is weak, let the blood be released gradually little by little according to his strength over consecutive days.
If one desires to drain the blood from his arm and to relieve its pressure, especially if he is robust in body, he should do so within seven or nine days after the first onset. However, if one wishes to draw the blood from his body towards the opposite side where it has congregated, then he should not proceed until the second or third day.
However, if the blood in one's body is copious and hot, causing fever, it is not permissible to let too much blood out at once; the opening should be gradually widened until it reaches the desired depth, ensuring all conditions for cupping are met. And place your hand on his pulse during the bleeding to prevent death at the site of the cupping, for this often occurs if the operator is ignorant or careless. It is not permissible to do so if you intend to relieve the arm and drain blood again.
If the vein's mouth becomes blocked and blood flow is slow, do not press hard or twist excessively; instead, either leave it until the next cupping session or use a lancet to gently scrape away any clotted blood blocking the vein's opening.
Alternatively, apply a solution of saltwater or carry out cupping with the help of Frankincense or Myrrh, gently expressing blood from the vein. If the vein becomes swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch him until the swelling subsides. If necessary, bloodletting must be done again, either at the same site or at another vein on the other arm.
For cupping the arm's vein, use a lancet instead of leeches if they are unavailable or hard to find, as the vein contains both blood and lymph. To perform cupping, immerse your hand in hot water until the skin reddens and veins become visible, then tie a bandage tightly but moderately above the site.
Then cup the vein slightly diagonally, neither horizontally nor vertically; make the opening wide. Perform cupping above the wrist joint. If blood does not flow out, reimmers your hand in the hot water and allow the blood to flow into it.
Until you reach your goal, if it is in the days of summer, you may dispense with immersing your hand in hot water again. Blood flow in hot water runs more swiftly during wintertime. But draining this vein is safer than all others. If it does not lie beneath a prominent vein or nerve, then cupping the wrist of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments. To perform this procedure, tie the arm with a bandage or press it with your hand while submerged in hot water until the vein swells.
If it does not lie beneath a prominent vein or nerve, then cupping the wrist of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments. To perform this procedure, tie the arm with a bandage or press it with your hand while submerged in hot water until the vein swells. It becomes evident to the touch, then cup it slightly askew. Even if you remove too much, it does not harm him. Be careful not to linger with the scalpel because beneath lies a nerve of the fingers and bare flesh.
Let blood flow freely. If you do not immerse it again in hot water, the blood will clot at the mouth of the vein and cease to flow. After removing enough blood, apply oil and salt to prevent rapid clotting.
And do the same with every narrow vein. As for cupping on the left hand, it is beneficial for spleen ailments and is done likewise as before. Cupping of the calf benefits lower body illnesses such as uterine problems.
And for retention of menses, kidney diseases, chronic sores on thighs and legs, and similar ailments. To perform cupping on the leg, immerse it in hot water and apply a massage until the veins appear. Then tie above the ankle joint with a bandage, and cup at the heel where the vein is prominent like the thumb.
And many veins branch out on the face of the foot. Therefore, cup at its widest vein or at the heel where they converge. If you cup on the sole of the foot, beware of the nerves underneath.
And perform cupping slightly askew, as if you intend to cut him. If blood does not flow out easily, immerse his leg again in hot water and let blood flow until it empties completely. If the cupper misses the vein on the first attempt,
It should be tied above with a ligature, slightly higher. For if the site is healthy, no harm is feared from it, provided care is taken not to damage the nerve, as we have said. The same applies to tying the vein on the other leg, regardless. As for the vein of the groin, its location is as we have mentioned, at the back of the thigh.
The benefit of tying the vein for pain in the hip is if it is due to hot blood. The method of tying: the patient should enter a warm bath and then quickly pull the leg from below the hip upwards towards the heel with four fingers using a thin, long cloth.
If it appears, then tie it in any case; either by displacing (the vein), which is better, or by amputating with a blade or incising. The site is safe and often quite deep; if you cannot find it.
If it does not appear at all, then tie some of its branches; those that are visible on the back of the foot near the little and ring fingers. Take care not to damage the nerves and allow only as much blood to flow out as desired.
Then release the pressure, apply a cloth to the site of the ligation, and press it. It will heal quickly.
Chapter Sixty-Nine.
Concerning cupping (hijama) and its method of use.
There are various types of cups used for cupping: they may be made of horns, wood, copper, or glass. There are two types of cupping procedures: one involving cauterization and bloodletting, the other without cauterization.
Of these two methods of cupping without cauterization...
...the nape of the neck, the occiput (back of the head); the hollow of the throat; the chin (underneath the lower jaw); the shoulders; the lumbar region of the back; the elbows; and the knees.
Cupping is used to draw blood from the veins that are distributed throughout the flesh. Unlike phlebotomy (venesection), it does not cause weakness. It should not be used with fire or without in cases of diseases involving fullness until the body has been completely evacuated.
If bloodletting is needed due to illness or habitually, we practiced it at any time, at the beginning, middle, or end of the month, regardless of the timing.
And among people, some feel heaviness and headache in their heads when they have too much blood that needs to be removed by bloodletting. Others experience fullness and redness in their faces, heads, and necks. Still others feel itching in their faces and behind their ears, darkness and blurring in their eyes, or they scratch the spot where they were cupped.
And among them, some laugh excessively. Others taste blood in their mouth and tear at their lips or vomit blood. Some sleep too much. And others see blood, redness, wounds, and surgeries in their dreams. Whenever we observe any of these things...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we commanded bloodletting after two or three hours have passed since noon. The benefit of cupping therapy for bleeding circles is that it relieves heaviness in the head and prevents water from flowing into the eyes.
However, it is preferable to perform this after purging the body completely. This bloodletting may be used instead of venesection (bloodletting from veins). It should not be used on those with a cold temperament or a recent illness, as it would cause them great harm.
Therefore, it is not advisable to use cupping therapy on the elderly, those with cold illnesses in their heads, or those who have become addicted to it and have lost their memory.
Therefore, it is advisable to command the cupping practitioner to slightly lower his hand with the cup downwards, fearing the onset of fainting. As for cupping on the nape of the neck, it serves as an alternative to venesection from the thigh and buttock.
Therefore, cupping is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, heaviness in the chest, coughing, and fullness. It is advisable to raise the cupping cup on the nape of the neck slightly, as it may cause weakness in the heart and stomach if pulled downwards.
As for cupping on the temples, it is beneficial for severe headaches, eye inflammation, migraines, neck pain, and toothache. It serves as an alternative to venesection from the buttock. It is advisable to command the cupping practitioner not to insert the lancet too deeply to avoid cutting a vessel and causing bleeding.
And as for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for treating ailments in the mouth such as dental abscesses and tooth decay. It serves as an alternative to venous bleeding from the cheeks. And cupping of the shoulders is beneficial for treating palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
And cupping on the buttocks is beneficial for what benefits bloodletting from the three large veins: basilic, temporal, and cubital. It draws blood from all the lesser vessels in the flesh, drawing those vessels until it reaches the three thick vessels.
It is necessary to command the cuppers not to dwell on the buttocks, for it is a naked place with nerves and vessels underneath. However, a single cupping cup placed on the spine is beneficial for hemorrhoids and lower abdominal ulcers.
And it is necessary to command the cuppers that the cups should be large and made of copper, for the place requires strong suction. Glass cups may break frequently, and they require many preparations. And cupping of the legs reduces swelling noticeably.
For it draws blood from the entire body, and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder. It regulates menstruation, treats boils and freckles, and serves as an alternative to phlebotomy of the elbows and knees, except that it greatly weakens the body and causes dizziness in most people. And cupping of the elbows has benefits similar to cupping of the legs.
The method of applying the cups is to first place them empty and create moderate suction, without lingering too long. Quickly apply and remove the cups to allow the humors to evenly flow into the site. Repeat this process until you see that the area has turned red and opened.
And when the blood has turned red, then you tighten and gradually release the suction. Then observe the state of the body; if the person is thin-skinned with loose pores, it is sufficient to perform cupping once only, so that the area does not become scarred.
The surgeon must dilate the incision slightly and deepen it, gently adjusting the stitches with careful movements. If blood is thick, he should perform the procedure twice; in the first instance to open a path for the free flow of blood and fluid, and in the second to ensure thorough removal of the thickened blood.
If the blood is very turbid, he should perform the procedure a third time to achieve the desired outcome. In summary, if one aims to remove only a small amount of blood, one incision will suffice; however, if one intends to remove a large quantity of blood, multiple incisions are necessary.
It is necessary to make the incision deep and moderate, extending only to the depth of the skin. It is not permissible for the surgeon to use ointments or waters at the time of cauterization; those with thick, hard, rough skin and narrow pores should be avoided.
It is not permissible to apply ointments to the cauterization sites, especially those that are opened or dissolved. However, if it is summer time, one may use oils such as olive oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or melon seed oil.
And if it is wintertime, oils such as narcissus oil, rose oil, frankincense oil, or saffron may be used. If the ointment is thick and cold, use oils like mercury oil, gall nut oil, butter, or wax, among others.
If the patient has large pores, soft flesh, and profuse sweating, it is not permissible to use ointments. These individuals should wash their cauterization sites after bloodletting with rose water, cold water, dog's grape juice, melon water, or pomegranate juice, among others. If the patient has moist, copious blood.
They should wash their cauterization sites with vinegar, water from the ash and sumac trees, or similar substances. If the ointment is thick, they should wash with old wine, mercury oil, pomegranate decoction, frankincense, or similar substances. Bloodletting should be avoided in hot baths and immediately after bathing.
It is preferable to use it after leaving the bathhouse by one or two hours, and no one should sleep afterwards. The bleeding cupper and assistant should prepare him before and after cupping.
First, one should look to see if the patient or assistant is yellowish. If so, it is preferable for them to consume cool substances such as pomegranates, spinach, vinegar, onions, and similar items. Their diet should consist of vegetables like lettuce, and light meats like mutton, fish, and similar foods.
For those with a cold disposition, they should drink honey water or rose syrup. They may also consume moderate perfumed wine and reduce their food intake. Their diet should consist of lettuce, broad beans, young pigeons, and sparrows. On the day of cupping and bloodletting, they should consume more liquid than solid food.
In some cases, it may be preferable for certain people to drink Farouq's treacle or musk medicine or silphium before cupping or bloodletting, and after that to strengthen the main organs and relax the blood. It should not be given to those with weakened constitutions.
As for cupping that is done without a restriction, it is performed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, heart, kidney area, and buttock. These organs do not tolerate restriction cups, as the intention is only to draw blood from one organ to another.
We place the cupping glass on the breasts for the treatment of engorgement, or use it to remove cold wind from an organ when it has lodged therein. We also place it on the abdomen and chest to loosen the organ, heat it, and relieve its pain by dissipating that wind.
Cupping may be applied to the kidneys if there is a blockage or stones present, as the force of suction may open the blockage or dislodge the stone from its position. The same applies when placed on the liver or spleen for wind obstruction. These cuppings are performed using empty cups with only mild suction.
It can be used with fire and can also be used filled with warm water for the treatment of swellings. This is done by filling the cupping glass, which should be large, with hot water alone or with water in which some herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Then it is placed filled on the spot and held, removed and repeated as many times as necessary. This is an image of the cupping glass that is used with fire:
The opening of its mouth is two fingers wide, as shown, and its depth is half a cubit. On one side of it there is a small hole the size of a needle's entrance. It is made of Chinese copper or yellow copper with thick rims.
It is smooth and flat, polished so as not to harm the limb when placed upon it. In its center there is a transverse tube of copper or iron where the candle is placed for fire. These cupping glasses can be made large or small according to the diseases.
And its use is that the cupping glasses for children and thin people are not like those for men. As for how to apply this cupping glass with fire on the limb, it is done by taking a tightly woven linen wick or a small wax candle made of pitch and placing it on the middle of the raised tube in the center of the cup.
So that the fire rises upwards towards the bottom of the cup without burning the patient's body. Then it is placed on the limb with the finger on the hole mentioned until, when we hold the cup, we no longer need to remove the finger.
And the steam comes out through that hole and the cup adheres to its place. Then the wick is extinguished as before if necessary. But the cupping glass used for treatment of swellings with water does not have a raised tube or a hole.
Rather, it is used by filling it with water and placing it on the limb. This is its image:
And the larger this cupping glass is to hold more water, the better.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
Regarding the treatment of hemorrhoids.
Ligation is used in most cases for members on which cups cannot be placed, either because they are too small like the lips and gums, or because the member is bare of flesh like fingers and nostrils.
The use of it is to take the leech that is found in fresh, pure waters from marshes, then leave it for a day and a night in fresh water until it starves and nothing remains inside its belly. Then evacuate the body first through cupping or bloodletting, then wipe the affected limb.
Until it turns red, then place it upon [the wound]; when it fills up and drops off, if possible, suck the spot with a leech; otherwise, wash the spot with vinegar then with much water, rub it, press it, and squeeze it. If bleeding continues after the leech falls off, that is considered purging. Then soak a cotton cloth in cold water.
Then place it above until the bleeding stops. If blood is abundant, sprinkle powdered gum arabic or myrrh or similar astringents upon it until bleeding stops; otherwise, place half-roasted barley flour on the spot.
And leave it until the barley flour adheres to the spot; if blood stops. If another leech is needed, do not reuse that one if possible; otherwise, wipe the spot with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the spot until some blood comes out, then place it; if it senses any blood, it will adhere to the site. If you wish for it to fall off, pour some myrrh or salt or ashes upon it.
Chapter Three.
Regarding Fractures.
This chapter also contains what is necessary for the art of medicine, namely: the reduction and setting of displaced fractures and dislocations of bones.
Know, O my sons, that many physicians who are ignorant and common people, as well as those who have never read any books of the ancients nor studied even a single letter from them, claim this chapter.
Therefore, this art has become extinct in our country, and I have never found anyone proficient in it at all. However, I benefited from what I did because of my extensive reading and study of the books of the ancients, and my persistent effort to understand them. Then, I adhered to practice and experience throughout my life.
I have drawn for you here all that my knowledge has encompassed. And I have gone over my experience in this, bringing it close to you, purifying it from verbosity, abbreviating it as much as possible, clarifying it as much as possible, and illustrating for you many images of the instruments used therein.
As it is an excess in explanation, like what was done in the preceding chapters. And there is no strength except with Allah the Most High.
The First Chapter.
In this section are summaries and comprehensive discussions about the matter of broken bones. And before we begin mentioning the fractured limbs, one by one, it is necessary to mention in the introduction of this chapter summaries without speeches. First of all, you must understand and grasp its true meaning, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble art.
So I say that whenever anyone breaks or dislocates or twists or falls, it is not permissible to hasten first to set or stretch or both. Unless there is something preventing that, such as weakness of strength, or if the person involved is a child, an old man, or very frail, or if the time is extremely hot or cold.
Then his diet should be restricted to cold legumes, poultry, and fish. He should avoid drinking beverages and consuming tough meats or foods that fill the veins with blood until the swelling has subsided and no more fluid is expected to accumulate at the site.
When that happens, the patient should return to his usual diet. If the fractured bone is being pushed into place, the patient should not consume foods that are very tough and sticky, like dates, rice, brains, lentils, or cow's hooves. And eggs, fried fish, thick beverages, and similar things. For by this method, the healing of the fracture will be quicker and better. Know that fractured bones in strong men and old people can never unite and adhere to their original nature.
Because the hardness and brittleness of their bones, although some may unite like the bones of young children. Nevertheless, nature produces on the broken bone something resembling glue that sticks and binds it together from all sides.
It binds itself together until it reaches its former state of strength and solidity, such that nothing hinders its actions.
Therefore, it is necessary to provide the patient with foods that are solid and viscous, as we have said. Know that fractures differ in type according to the variation of limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone differs from that of the skull, and a fracture of the rib cage differs from that of the spine.
Similarly, all other limbs differ from one another. We will come to mention each type of fracture in its respective chapter, detailed and distinct from others; for the type of bone fracture may also vary.
For a fracture may occur without splinters, or it may be along the length of the bone with both sharp and blunt fragments; it may also occur accompanied by wounds and lacerations of the skin, or as a slight crack.
Each type has its own specific method of treatment, as will be mentioned in detail later. Among the indications for bone fracture is its deformity, numbness, prominence to touch, and cracking sound upon pressure with the hand.
However, if there is no apparent deformity, no cracking sound upon pressure, no disturbance felt when touching the bone, and the patient does not complain of much pain, then it is not a fracture but rather a sprain, slight fracture, or minor crack. In such cases, do not manipulate or press the limb excessively.
But rather, apply to it the medications that will be mentioned later, which are appropriate for its location. Then gently traction is applied. Know that when a bone fractures into two pieces without any splinters, each piece completely separates from the other.
It is not permissible for you to rush into reducing and immobilizing it until the inflammatory swelling has subsided. If an inflammatory swelling occurs, leave it alone until it subsides, then reduce and immobilize it with gentleness and skill.
Know that reducing and immobilizing a bone with splinters is easier than one without. Then proceed as will be mentioned later. If the bone has splinters, it is necessary to traction the fractured limb from both sides, whether it is an arm or a leg.
If the limb is small, then use your hand; otherwise, use two threads or both thread and hand. Ensure the limb's position is level and in its natural shape until the fractured bone extends. Then, gently push back the protruding parts into their original places with care and skill.
Strive to cause no pain or discomfort to the patient through your actions. Aim to join one bone to its companion in the best possible way, and at this time you should examine and feel them with your hand. If you find anything amiss, rectify it to the best of your ability.
And beware of excessive pressure and forceful squeezing, as many unskilled individuals do. They often speak of having done so, but this may result in burning or cauterization of the limb, which I have seen happen repeatedly.
Then, after the bone has been aligned and secured, instruct the patient to keep it still and at rest. Warn them not to move it during wakefulness or sleep, or during any turning, disturbance, or stretching, and advise them to use a splint that prevents pain.
And if the patient feels pain or discomfort with the current splint, they should shift to another that does not cause pain, ensuring that it remains straight and level.
Otherwise, the limb may become deformed upon healing. As for how to splint a broken limb, know that they differ in size and shape, so small ones like arms, fingers, hands, etc.
Therefore, for small limbs, the bandages should be soft and pliable, while for larger ones like thighs, back, or chest, they should not be too tight as this would constrict the limb evenly on all sides without causing any harm.
Once you have finished aligning the bone, begin applying the appropriate plaster cast. Gently place the plaster on the fracture site, then begin wrapping the bandage around it three or four times, depending on what the limb requires. Tighten your hand lightly with the bandage, then bring it up to the upper side of the fracture.
Bind slightly less than your usual tension on the broken site, then withdraw with winding somewhat from the fracture site and loosen gradually little by little until you obtain some suitable material from the correct site. Then take another bandage and wrap it around the broken site as well.
Wrap it several times, then proceed with winding to the lower side of the fracture. Ensure that your action in tightening and loosening the wrapping matches what was mentioned earlier regarding the upper first wrap. Then insert between the wrappings a piece of soft linen or cloth sufficient to straighten the fracture.
If there is no deformity, do not add anything. Then wrap another bandage around it and secure it with these well-made wrappings from your clock if the limb has neither swelling nor heat.
If there is swelling or heat, apply something to alleviate the swelling and reduce the inflammation. Leave it for some time, then tighten the splints upon it at that point. The splints should be made of half-dried reeds, skillfully prepared, or they may be made of ebony wood.
Which are made from pinewood or palm leaves, rushes, or reeds and the like that are readily available. Each splint should be of this same size and shape.
Except for the splint placed directly on the fracture, which should be slightly thicker and wider than the others. As for the length of the splint, it should be made according to the appropriate size of the broken limb, its picture is as follows:
Then secure the splints with another bandage according to your original tension, then tie it firmly from above with strong threads as mentioned earlier in binding, ensuring that your tension on the fracture site is greater. The further away from the fracture, the less tension there should be.
The threads should be of medium thickness and fineness, preferably made of moist cotton thread, as using coarse threads like those seen from ignorant people who use thick strips of cotton cloth is a great mistake.
For it falls short externally of moderation, and the linen threads are also unsuitable because you cannot achieve what you want by pulling with them. It is not proper that there be less than a finger's breadth between one stitch and another.
If the patient is harmed by the ends of the sutures after pulling in the correct places, then place beneath them a piece of linen or soft wool padding until no harm comes from it. But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will come to that separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be sutured on the first day. For if it is large, stitches should not be placed until after five or seven days, or more, depending on whether there is fear of inflammation.
This is the description of the bandages with which the ancients used to dress fractures and dislocations, a general concise dressing that fixes the fracture and suits most temperaments, especially children and women, because it does not incline towards heat or cold.
Take from the flour dust that adheres to the walls of the millstone when it is moved by the millstone, mix it as it is without beating with egg whites and make its mixture neither too thick nor too thin, then use it.
This is another dressing for fractures, dislocations and sprains. Take ten dirhams of each of the following: barley groats, oatmeal, aniseed, nigella seeds, frankincense and saffron; five dirhams each of myrrh and incense; twenty dirhams of olive oil; and twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman earth. Grind all of these together and mix with olive oil water or egg whites if the patient's temperament is hot. Then use this dressing, for it is well-composed and quickly sets broken bones, suitable for most people due to its balance.
This is another dressing that also benefits fractures and sprains. Take ten dirhams each of frankincense, barley groats and white barley flour; five dirhams each of myrrh and incense; six dirhams of aniseed; and twenty dirhams of Armenian earth. Grind all finely, sift, mix with water or egg whites and use.
A compress for dislocated joints and broken bones that have shifted from their places, to alleviate sudden pain and bone fractures, as well as headache. White wool is taken, soaked in vinegar and cooked oil, and placed on the location. This compress has no healing power.
A compress for cooling hot swellings and relieving pains, especially. Another compress to heal broken bones: dates leaves and bramble leaves are taken together, crushed, and applied wet. Another shorter compress used when a bone is forced out of joint.
To analyze the remainder of a tumor, take equal parts of aloeswood, papyrus, and violet leaves, grind them all together, mix with plaster if the organ is not inflamed; if it is inflamed, use water from coriander or water instead.
Another stronger compress for analyzing solid tumors when bones are displaced: take equal parts of aloeswood, bdellium, colocynth, garland flower, rue, violet leaves, and papyrus.
Grind all together and mix with vinegar water, fresh water, or plaster according to the organ's heat and its quietness. Hippocrates did not mention applying it directly to the broken member when healing.
Except for the Quruttiyy compress made of wax and oil alone, which he described to be medium between hardness and softness. Galen saw that things with dryness and some heat, like myrrh, ginger, and frankincense (as mentioned earlier), should be placed on the broken member during healing.
A compress beneficial for weakness and pain: take equal parts of nard, sesame, human hair or bird feathers, aloeswood, and salt. Grind, sieve, mix, and apply as a compress. As for the amount of bandage that should remain...
...then examine it. If no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone occurs, do not change it often; but if severe itching, intense pain, or swelling appear...
Hurry to lanced it at the right time, do not delay that at all. Then apply a bandage to it and then take a soft cloth or sea sponge moistened in warm water and wash the place until the itching subsides and the pain ceases. Then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then wrap it with white wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rose ointment and tie it overnight to ensure the hot swelling subsides, reduce member inflammation and alleviate its pain. Then resume gentle traction and light bandaging, not as strong as the first one.
And treat it until it heals. If you find that the swelling, redness, pain, inflammation and all symptoms have completely disappeared and you need to resume bandaging and traction, then repeat as originally done. Otherwise, do nothing further if no symptoms mentioned occur in the member.
Do not lanced it again until three or four or five or seven days have passed, and sometimes it may be left for twenty days depending on the appearance of the member as mentioned earlier. Until when you find that it is necessary to break open and flesh has formed upon it.
Then at that time increase traction more than the original one altogether and also increase the thickness of the invalid's food as mentioned earlier. If you find that the site of fracture has dried up and wasted more than it should, then know that nutrition is prevented from reaching it.
Then rinse it with warm water every time you lanced it, doing this once every three days and slightly reduce traction. For by this action nutrition is brought to the member and it heals quickly. But as for what ignorant people do in breaking the member again.
If they did not originally set it properly and then reset it crookedly, this is a mistake on their part and a great error. If it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it, but I have not seen anyone do it at all. And the correct thing to do is not to follow that practice.
Chapter Two.
On fractures of the head.
Types of fractures that occur in the head are many and varied, with diverse shapes and intricate causes. Among these fractures is one caused by a sword blow; this may either break all the bone until it reaches the peritoneum beneath the bone. And as it does in wood.
So this type of fracture is called 'qadumi', and if the sword cuts only part of the bone, breaking its surface but not penetrating all the way through, it is called a 'sulaiba' fracture. The wound in both types may be large or small.
Another type of fracture occurs from a blow with stone or a fall onto stone, appearing as a bruise or laceration. This fracture may either penetrate close to the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be located on the surface of the bone.
Similarly, the wound in these fractures can also be large or small. Another type of fracture is hidden within the bone like a hair's breadth, causing dizziness.
And this type of fracture is called 'sha'iri'. Another type occurs from a fall or blow with stone, entering the surface of the bone like a coin into copper.
Such fractures are more common in children with soft skulls. In all these types of fractures, there may be sharp or blunt fragments. We will discuss the treatment of each type later. Now we will describe the examination to identify these types of fractures.
And the examination involves using tweezers and removing rotten flesh. The 'sha'iri' type can be identified by wiping the bone with ink; if there is a fracture, it will appear black. As for treatment, first consider the patient's symptoms.
If you observe symptoms that strongly indicate fear, such as vomiting, prostration, loss of consciousness, sudden onset of fever, dilation and redness of the eyes, do not approach or treat the patient, for death is imminent in most cases with these symptoms.
But if you see signs that do not cause concern and hope for recovery, then take the following measures to treat him: If the injury occurs during winter...
It is necessary to strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and during the summer days it is necessary to hasten its removal before the seventh day, lest what lies beneath the bone from the membrane should rot, thus exposing those symptoms which we have described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane surrounding the brain and is accompanied by wounding and inflammation, it is necessary to cut off the shattered and infected part as I have described for you, which is to shave the head of the injured person.
And when you expose the bone, proceed however and according to the shape of the wound and what is apparent to the patient. If upon revealing the bone there is bleeding or a hot swelling, then respond accordingly.
And that you stuff the place with cloth dipped in syrup and honey and rose water, and sew up the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding is secured. Then proceed to cut and remove the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first way, it is to cut the bone with a fine narrow-bladed knife, and this is its appearance:
Then use another blade after this one, slightly wider than the previous one. This is its appearance:
And also use another blade, even wider than the second one. And you should have a variety of blades with some being wider and others shorter, all with very sharp edges, made of Indian iron or good steel.
And use gentleness when striking the blade so as not to shake the head and cause injury. If the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to drill around it before using the blades with the drills called 'non-recessed drills'.
Because these drills do not exceed the thickness of the cranial bone beyond it, as their tip is rounded like a collar or small circle which prevents them from penetrating too deeply and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And you should have many of these drills, each suitable for a certain thickness of bone. Thus, you will have drills for every skull with tips that are long and short according to the thickness of the skull. Here are three types: Of trepanations, there are large, medium, and small:
And as for the manner of drilling around the broken bone, it is to place the trepan on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been perforated, then move the trepan to another location and leave a space between each drill equal to the thickness of a barley grain or thereabouts.
Then cut with the saw what is between each two drill holes, and do so with as much gentleness as you can, as we have said, until you remove the bone either by hand or with some other tool prepared for that purpose.
Like the forceps and gentle tongs, and it is necessary to be very careful that the trepan or saw does not touch the peritoneum. If you hollow out the bone and free it from the peritoneum if it was adhered to it, and remove it, then do not strip [the bone].
And smooth the roughness that remains in the bone with another tool resembling the saw, but it should be finer and gentler than other saws. If there are any small bones or fragments left, remove them gently with whatever tools you have.
Then treat the wound afterwards with bandaging and ointments, as mentioned. As for the other side of the operation, it is an easy matter far from danger, as Galen mentions and praises greatly; and this is his saying: It is necessary for you first to begin by exposing a part of the bone from the place where the fracture is most severe and prominent, until when you expose that part you see beneath it the end of this lentil-shaped saw, which looks like this:
The lentil-shaped part of it is smooth and does not cut anything, while the sharp part is on its sides extending lengthwise as you see. Thus, the lentil-shaped part rests on the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the saw is on the bone, then strike the saw from one side only.
With a small hammer until you cut through the bone gently as it turns, and you are safe from harm to the membrane even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and weak among people indeed even if he is blind.
If there remains something attached to the bone from the membrane in some places of the bone, scrape it off gently with the pointed end of the lentil-shaped probe and remove it carefully, for it will detach without harm or fear.
And if a bone fracture did not reach the membrane and what was broken off from the bone is its face, with small fragments and rough edges remaining within it, then you should remove those rough edges and extract those fragments using delicate forceps of various sizes that have been prepared for this purpose, so that you may choose from among them whatever fits best in each situation according to the requirements of the task.
And in the case of a broken bone and its shape, you should first use wider forceps to grip the bone, then proceed to use narrower ones gradually, continuing in this manner until you reach the use of the finest and thinnest ones.
And as for small cracks and minor fractures, you should treat each one according to what is appropriate for its repair, which is something that is not hidden from anyone who has even the slightest training in this field and who has paid attention to what we have written and demonstrated regarding major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, then take a piece of cotton cloth appropriate for the wound and dip it in rose oil, place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another folded or triangular piece of cloth. And dip it in honey and rose oil and place it on top of the first cloth, doing so with great gentleness to avoid weighing down the intestines, then use a wide bandage from above without pulling too tightly except enough to hold the wound closed.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the inflammation has subsided, then remove it and afterwards use powders made from beneficial substances such as onion roots, frankincense flour, almonds, and celery seeds, among others.
And sprinkle them dry on the wound as they are, and in general, use a medication that has the property of cleaning without causing inflammation. Be sure to keep your treatment of the wound clean at all times, avoiding oiliness, dirt, and not allowing pus to accumulate.
For pus, when it collects on the membrane of the brain, corrupts and inflames it, causing great harm to the patient. It may also appear in the back of the head, especially if its treatment is neglected.
Blackening of the surface [of an ulcer], if it appears, indicates that the patient has exhibited the symptoms we mentioned. Know then that he is doomed without doubt, and that blackening occurred due to a medication applied to it; that medication had the power to cause such blackening.
It is necessary to take one part of honey and three parts of rose oil, mix them well, and use it to stain a cloth. Then place the cloth on the peritoneum [of the abdomen] and treat him with appropriate remedies until he recovers.
Chapter Three.
How to set a broken nose.
Know that the nose does not break except for its upper parts, which are both bones. The lower part is made of cartilage and does not break; instead it suffers from deformity, dislocation, or fracture.
If one of its sides breaks, insert the little finger into the nostril and push the bone back into place with your index finger from inside and thumb from outside until you restore the nose to its natural shape. Do this gently so that you do not cause pain to the patient.
If the break is at the top of the nose and you cannot reach it with your finger, use a thin, slightly thickened rod to push the bone back into place. If both sides are broken, do the same for each side. Perform reduction on the first day after the fracture occurs.
If it is not possible [to reduce the fracture on the first day], do so on the seventh or tenth day after the swelling has subsided. Then insert a wick of cotton into the nostril if one side is broken, or two wicks if both sides are broken.
And let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early reducers mention that the wicks should be lubricated with oil and changed daily, but I do not recommend this. Rather, the wicks should be dipped in beaten egg white mixed with flour dust.
Then leave the splint until the bone hardens and the cartilage calcifies, and you may insert into the nose the place of the splint goose feather tubes after wrapping them with soft gauze so that their retention would be stronger and to prevent the patient from breathing difficulty.
And this is not necessary if you wish to do it, and if you wish to make the splints. If during your work there appears a swelling or inflammation in the nose, apply cotton soaked in rose oil or honey and deer fat, or some diacylon ointment.
If no inflammation appears, do not apply anything from outside with semolina flour and almond dough mixed with egg whites, then place a soft compress without binding the nose at all. Otherwise, if the bones of the nose break slightly.
Otherwise, if the bones of the nose are broken or crushed, it is necessary to incise them and extract them with the appropriate instrument, then suture the incision and treat it with appropriate healing ointments. If there is an injury inside the nose, it should be treated with splints and lead pipes used. Until it heals.
Chapter Four
If the lower lip is broken.
If the lower lip is broken without being cut, and if it is only broken externally without being divided into two and becoming displaced internally, its recognition is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, insert the left hand's index finger into the patient's mouth and if it is on the left side, insert the right hand's index finger. Then gently lift the fracture prominence from inside out with it.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, guide its straightening. If the jawbone fracture has been broken into two pieces, then use a lever from both sides to ensure its proper alignment. If any teeth have been displaced.
Or if teeth have been separated, tie those that you want to remain in place with gold, silver, or brass wire. Then apply cotton soaked in rose oil on the broken lip, then place a folded gauze on it and finally place a tight bandage or a piece of leather strap equal to the length of the lip. Then you bind it above according to what you prefer, securing the bandage until no leakage occurs. You instruct the patient to rest quietly and give them soft, light food. If you notice any change in appearance on any side within three days, hasten to remove it.
Then you bind it above according to what you prefer, securing the bandage until no leakage occurs. You instruct the patient to rest quietly and give them soft, light food. If you notice any change in appearance on any side within three days, hasten to remove it. Then you treat what you have wrapped with flour ground with egg white or fine barley meal after removing the catgut from it. Apply a soft pad on top, leaving it until the bandage adheres to him and no change occurs in the bone's condition.
Do not remove it until the wound heals and the fracture solidifies. Often, this fracture becomes firm within three weeks. If inflammation occurs during this time, apply the previously mentioned remedies repeatedly to subside the swelling until it disappears.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, observe: if any bone fragments have become detached, gently remove them using any suitable instrument. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with scissors according to your need.
Then, after removing those fragments and none remain, suture the mouth of the wound if it is wide; otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for that purpose. And heal the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five
**On Reduction of a Dislocated Shoulder.**
Most commonly, the collarbone fractures at its anterior end near the elbow, and this fracture occurs in one of three directions: either it breaks and splits into two without any fragments, which is the easiest to reduce.
Or it may occur with fragments, which is more difficult to reduce. If the fracture occurs without a wound, prepare two assistants: one to hold the arm that lies next to the fractured collarbone, and another to extend the neck towards the other side.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until it assumes its proper shape, without any displacement or shortening. If you need to extend further, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
It should fit the size of your need and extend, raise the collarbone, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture aligns correctly. If you are unable to pull the tip of the collarbone outward because it has receded deeply, then the patient should lie on their back.
A moderate-sized pad is placed under the shoulder blade and the servant presses down on it until the recessed collarbone rises above. Then, with your fingers, fix and align the fracture.
If you feel that a fragment of the collarbone has broken off and is moving around, do not cut into it to remove the fragment. If the fragment is stuck in the bone, then change your plan and use one of the prepared saws to cut it. After placing the apparatus that holds the diaphragm beneath the collarbone - this apparatus is made of wood or iron and looks like this:
It resembles a spoon without curvature, its width should match the size of the bone you need to work on, and its length should be suitable for you to operate with. It has two ends, one broader and the other narrower.
If the wound you made to remove the bone fragment is wide and you are confident that there will be no bleeding, then sew the edges of the wound. If it is narrow or if you fear bleeding, then close the wound with stitches and strips according to its size.
If there is heavy bleeding, apply pressure on the wound using rose oil and vinegar or wine. If the bone needs setting without cutting or wounding it, then use a bandage made from flour paste mixed with egg white. Then place the padded sling on it, and put a ball under his armpit if needed. Then take a long strip of cloth, about an inch wide, fold it into a bandage, place it on the sling and dressing, then take a splint made from thin board. The bandage should be three fingers wide and of the same length. Then insert it into a cloth, then insert this cloth with the splint into the appropriate place on the bandage that corresponds to the fracture site. Then tie the bandage around the fracture as tightly as possible. And thus on his neck and under his armpit, both healthy and sick, rubbing it repeatedly on every side. And you have seen that traction corrects the fracture perfectly, something which is not hidden from you.
And all of your understanding should be that the plaster bandage should not be removed from the broken bone. So you should visit the patient every day, for whenever the bandage loosens and you see that the plaster has fallen off, then repair it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on his back with a small pillow under his armpit at night.
Raise his arm with it (the pillow) from his side so that the fracture of the collarbone is raised along with the shoulder joint, or tie his arm to his neck. Do not untie the bandage unless there is an incident such as itching or swelling in that place for twelve days.
Then renew the bandage if you see fit to do so. And keep rubbing it (the bandage) until it becomes firm and the fracture of the collarbone solidifies and becomes stronger in twenty-eight days. Some people may take less than that time.
Chapter Six.
Chapter Six: Setting a shoulder fracture. Rarely does the shoulder blade itself break, but rather its angles do. When it breaks at one of its points or in the middle, this is known by touch. According to how the fracture appears, so shall be its reduction.
Set it (the bone) back into its natural shape with whatever means you have available, then apply a poultice of millstone dust mixed with egg whites and linseed oil to the area. Then place a folded cloth bandage on top of that, followed by a wide splint made of wood.
As thin as the shoulder itself or slightly wider. If there is any swelling under the bandage in one of the areas of the shoulder, smooth it out with linseed oil until the bandage lies flat, then tie it all together with a long strip of cloth.
Tie it firmly and make sure the bandage does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day, tightening it whenever it becomes loose. If the bandage falls off, reposition it. The patient should lie on his sound side with his shoulder immobilized for twenty days.
After twenty or twenty-five days, once this period has passed, then cut the suture and you will be safe. It is from the bones which cause no fear of breaking nor concern about their healing. If a splinter of bone protrudes and presses beneath the skin, incise over it and remove it. And perform what was mentioned regarding fracture of the collarbone to alleviate the hot swelling if such a thing occurs.
Chapter Seven
In treating fractures of the chest.
The breastbone may fracture in its middle, but this happens rarely; rather, its extremities are more prone to fracturing and splintering. One of the symptoms of a middle fracture is that it inclines downward and causes severe pain with difficulty breathing and coughing.
And perhaps blood may be coughed up, and the cracking sound in the broken bone is noticeable to the touch. Its treatment involves having the patient lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulders, then pressing down on their elbows and bringing together the ribs with both hands while gently smoothing them out as much as possible.
And continue until the bone returns to its proper shape, then apply a bandage and splint before placing on top of it a light plaster cast made from thin slices of willow or broomstick, wrapped in cloth. Then carefully bind it to the broken bone so that it does not slip off, wrap the bandage around the back in a circular motion several times and tie it gently, then check the bandage regularly, tightening it whenever it becomes loose.
And if necessary to remove it due to pain or swelling in the area, then hurry up and remove the bandage, treat whatever has appeared with its appropriate treatment, and then replace the bandage if necessary. Face him [the patient] and keep pulling until it heals.
Chapter Eight
In treating the ribs if they are fractured, know that the fracture of the ribs occurs only in the hard parts that follow the back and their extremities at the front. They are vulnerable to fractures because they are cartilaginous, and this is evident upon examination with the fingers.
She should align the fracture with her fingers on the practiced face until the shape is as it should be, then bandage and secure the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If it's an inward-slanting rib fracture, it will expose the lungs to severe pain.
And like the cough that afflicts those with it, causing the bone to pierce the pleura and leading to difficulty in breathing, coughing up much blood. This is difficult to treat; many remedies have been tried for it.
Some say it is appropriate to make the patient's diet produce wind and gases, causing the abdomen to swell and pushing out the fracture. We disapprove of this, lest it cause a hot swelling if none has occurred, or worsen it if one already exists.
Some advise placing a cup on the site and applying suction, similar to cupping except that one fears the cup may adhere due to its weakness. Others say it is appropriate to cover the site with wool soaked in hot oil.
The space between the ribs becomes filled so that the bandage is level when wrapped around, then treat the cough with appropriate food and medicine. If the patient's condition is severe and unbearable, however... It was feared that the bone would pierce the pleura, causing harm to the patient. Therefore, it is appropriate to cut open the site and expose the broken rib, then place beneath it the apparatus for protecting the abdomen that has been mentioned earlier, and gently cut and remove the bone.
Then, if the wound is large, close it with stitches and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during this time, quickly apply warm compresses made of rose oil to the site, and also treat the patient internally with remedies that alleviate swellings.
He should lie on the side where sleep is easier until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
How to set dislocated vertebrae and neck.
If the neck or spine suffers a fracture, which rarely happens, and more commonly a dislocation (luxation) occurs. To determine whether it has healed... If you see that his hands have become paralyzed and numb, lifeless and unable to move or stretch out or close, and if he does not feel any pain when pricked with a needle, then know that recovery is unlikely in most cases; he is lost.
If he can move them and feels pain upon pricking or pressing, know that the marrow of his bones is unharmed and that the patient may recover with treatment. However, if this condition affects the spine, you should determine whether recovery is possible.
If you examine his legs, and find that they have become paralyzed just like his hands, then if he lies on his back wind and feces escape without control, and if he lies on his stomach urine escapes without control. If he lies on his back and cannot control urine, know that he is beyond hope; do not bother with treatment. However, if this does not happen, the condition is less severe. Treat it by applying a poultice to soothe the hot swelling.
Apply rose oil alone or with burned garlic cloves to the affected area three times a day until the inflammation subsides. Then apply a wet compress and bind it tightly, instructing him to remain still and quiet.
He should sleep only on the side that does not cause him pain until he recovers. If a bone fragment or foreign body has entered during birth and healed, do not cut into the skin to remove it. Then close the wound with stitches if it is large, and treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last vertebra of the spine has broken off (the tailbone), do not insert your left thumb into the anus.
Set the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible, then apply a splint and bandage if necessary, binding it tightly.
If you feel a broken bone fragment inside, cut into the wound, remove it, and treat the injury as described previously until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
Force bone fracture in hip joint.
Most cases of hip joint fractures heal well. However, if it does happen, the fracture will be such that the ends of the bone shatter and crack lengthwise, causing the limb to lean inward and expose the sciatic nerve to pain and numbness in the affected leg.
If a fracture occurs, run your hand over it until you reach the break. If the ends of the bone are shattered only at their tips, align them according to what seems fit until they resemble their natural shape.
If the fracture runs lengthwise or has leaned inward, lay the patient on their stomach to align the bone. Once aligned, apply a splint and then place a wooden or leather bandage, securing it with binding.
Do not fear displacement of the fracture or loosening of the bandage. Fill in any gaps between the toes with material to ensure even pressure and prevent discomfort. Have the patient sleep on their back or unaffected side, if swelling occurs.
Stop compressing the fracture and align it until the swelling subsides. Apply a dressing to alleviate discomfort, as mentioned earlier. Then return to aligning and securing the fracture with binding, but do not attempt to remove any bone fragments or chips at its ends.
Do not touch it but align it from outside as mentioned before, leaving it bound until it heals.
Chapter Eleven
On the reduction of fractures of the arm.
The elbow is between the elbow joint and the shoulder joint. If fractured, reduce it in one of two ways: take a smooth, well-polished piece of wood like this:
And tie the ends with ropes. Then suspend it from an elevated point, have the patient sit on a chair and place their fractured arm over the wooden bar so that their armpit touches its center of curvature. Then hang something heavy from above to immobilize the arm.
Or, a servant can extend it downward while the physician manually reduces the fracture with both hands until the bone returns to its proper position. Alternatively, the patient can lie on their back and hang their arm from their neck using a rope.
Then order two servants: one to hold above the fracture with his hands, and another to hold below it and extend each towards his side. And if you wish for stronger traction, tie beneath the fracture with a bandage and above it with another, and then extend each from their sides by the servants.
If the fracture is close to the elbow, it is preferable for one bandage to be placed under the armpit and the other under the fracture near the forearm. Similarly, if the fracture is near the wrist, the bandages should be placed on that spot and on the wrist itself.
Then gently align the fracture without force until it fits properly and heals well. Then, if there is no hot swelling, tighten it; but if there is hot swelling, leave it untightened for seven days, then apply a compress of moistened lint soaked in vinegar and anointed with rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it. The method of tightening is: lay a pad on the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth around the pad, bring the arm to rest on its own upper arm and place the hand flat on the elbow.
And wrap the cloth and the bandage around the upper arm and forearm, so that the arm acts as a splint if there is no impediment preventing this. If you fear any change in the bone, then use splints.
This is to place a plaster on the fracture itself, wider and stronger than other plasters. Leave one finger's width between each plaster, and make the length of the plasters according to the fracture plus three fingers' width from both sides.
Then tighten the splints with the bandage mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And make your tightening on the site of the fracture stronger.
The further from the fracture, the less tight should be the bandage. If you see that setting the splints and bandaging at this time is appropriate for your patient's condition, do so accordingly.
If you fear hot swelling, leave the bandage and splints for seven days as mentioned earlier. Then examine the bandage every three days to ensure that there is no irritation or swelling at the site, nor restriction of blood flow to the limb due to excessive tightness. All this should be done as described previously.
If you are confident that nothing will happen, then do not cut the ligature except after many days. The patient should lie on his back with his hand on his abdomen, and a bolster filled with wool, moderate in size, should be placed under his armpit.
And examine at all times, day or night, lest the shape of the broken limb should change or the ligature should relax, and then set it right with your own effort. And make the patient's food according to what has been mentioned, so that it is soft at first, until the bone begins to harden.
It is not permissible to make the patient's food coarse, for it is customary for the arm and leg to harden in forty days. At that time, it becomes necessary to cut the ligature, use the bathhouse, and treat with appropriate ointments, especially if the fracture is comminuted.
Do not remove the ligature until fifty days have passed or two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On Setting Fractures of the Arm.
The arm consists of two bones, the radius and ulna. One is called 'the small bone', which lies next to the thumb, while the other is called 'the large bone' which underlies it from below. Sometimes only the larger bone may be fractured, or sometimes only the smaller one, and sometimes both.
When the upper end of the radius is fractured, its reduction is easy and its exposure is swift. But when the lower end of the ulna is fractured, its reduction is difficult and its exposure is slow, and most difficult of all if both bones are fractured.
It is necessary for the physician, when reducing the fracture, to do so gently and easily until it is aligned. If the ulna is the fractured bone, he should apply more pressure. But if both bones are fractured, he must exert great force.
It is necessary to place the hand in the correct position during reduction, with the arm extended on a pillow. The thumb should be elevated above all the fingers and the little finger should be lower than the others. The patient should sit upright.
The pillow should be at his level to prevent discomfort for the patient.
Then a servant stretches the arm from below, either by hand or with a bandage, and another servant does likewise from above. The doctor then aligns the bone until it is properly repositioned; if there are any splintered fragments, they should be removed.
If any fragment remains out of place, try to return it. If there is an exposed splinter that cannot be reduced and is piercing the skin, make an incision over it and extract it according to the method described earlier. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, this case has its own chapter.
Then, take treatment from there. If swelling and heat appear at the start of reduction, apply a compress made of cotton soaked in a mixture of rose oil and white wax; it should be moderate between thickness and softness. Apply it gently until the swelling subsides.
