Then remove the cotton and apply the prepared bandage made from wheat flour and egg white. Apply the splints, ensuring that the one on the fracture itself is slightly broader and stronger. Know that in most cases there are six splints for the arm.
If the fracture is in one elbow or both elbows, tie the bandage tightly and firmly at the site of the fracture. Whenever you wrap the bandage upwards or downwards, make it slightly looser than mentioned earlier.
And let the bandage that is wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not too firm. The thread used to tie it should be made of cotton, moderate between fineness and coarseness as described earlier. Examine the limb and its ligaments after some days.
If something needs to be corrected, such as itching that appears on the limb, do not immerse the limb in warm water until the itching subsides. Leave the limb loose overnight so that it may rest, then resume binding.
If the bandage has loosened and the bone has shifted or something similar has occurred, fix it all as much as possible. Also, if nourishment is not reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, loosen the bandage slightly for a few days until nutrition reaches it, then reapply.
If no improvement is seen by the patient regarding what we have mentioned, it is not permissible to remove [the splint] except after twenty days or thereabouts. Then tie the patient's hand to his neck, let his arm be balanced and he should avoid sudden movements, and let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm is healed in thirty days or forty-three days, and perhaps it may heal in twenty-eight days, all according to the conditions of temperaments [humors] and strengths.
Chapter Thirteen.
In setting fractures of the hand and fingers.
Fractures of the palm and dislocations of the fingers are rarely seen, but contusions are common. Therefore, if a fracture or contusion occurs in the palm...
It is necessary for the patient to sit cross-legged with two chairs placed flat before him.
Then he places his hand on it [the chair] extended, then the servant of bonesetter stretches it out and the physician aligns it until it unites nicely. Then at this point, it is necessary to apply a splint and bandage if there is no heat present in the swelling. After that, a plaster cast is applied according to the location.
And having wrapped it in a soft cloth, if the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, make a ball-like shape out of the cloth and command the patient to grip it with his fractured hand. Then pull it with a long cloth.
And let the plaster cast be of soft leather that can conform to the skin while allowing full flexion of the hand. If the fracture is on the outside, then make a plaster cast from above and another from below the hand to keep it elevated and open.
Then apply the traction as the hand rotates and tie it between the fingers. If the fracture is in one of the phalanges, if it is the thumb, align it properly then bind it with the hand. If you wish to make a small plaster cast for it to keep the fracture in place.
And do not move him. If the fracture is in one of the other fingers, such as the middle, index, ring or little finger, align it and bind it with the neighboring finger that is straight or bind all of them together on a splint which is better, or place a small plaster cast on it.
As we have mentioned regarding the thumb, examine it frequently; apply pressure after it becomes hot and swollen. Then treat it as appropriate when such a condition occurs, as has been repeatedly described.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
In setting fractures of the thigh.
The thighbone often breaks and it is felt because it twists forward and backward. Its reduction should be done by pulling one ligament above the fracture and another below it, with the patient lying on his face.
Then, every assistant ligament should be extended towards its side gently, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the hip, a stronger ligament made of wool or similar material should be used to pull.
At the hip's origin towards the pubis, one ligament should be pulled upwards and the other below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the ligament should be close to the knee with the pull directed downwards. Then, the physician should adjust the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone should be well set. Then, the splint and bandage should be applied. If there is no heat in the limb, then apply them immediately. But if there is heat, leave it alone until the heat subsides, then return to treatment.
As for binding it, a wide, stiff bandage should be wound around it twice or thrice without touching the fracture. Then, twist the leg until the heel is at the hip's root, and insert a long thread between the thigh and leg near the knee from below, with both ends of the thread above.
Then, wind the remaining bandage around both the leg and thigh. Place splints on the thigh at the fracture site and make them into one splint for the leg bone. Then, stuff the gap between the thigh and leg with soft cloth to distribute the tension evenly.
Then, begin strong pulling from the middle towards the fracture site with three or four turns. As you move away from the fracture, make your pulls lighter and gentler.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread which you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie it with what is adjacent from above, then pass its end downward until it reaches the man's anus, then also draw tight both ends of the sutures from the other side.
Lest the suture slip from its place, then leave it untied until there is no talk of inflammation or swelling or edema etc., for if anything of that occurs, hasten to release it and repair whatever has happened thereof as mentioned previously.
But if there is a splinter in the bone that has become embedded, then do not attempt to reduce it if you are able; otherwise, cut upon it and extract it, and treat the wound as mentioned previously until it heals. This fracture may also cause stiffness of the thigh without any sutures being added to the leg.
As mentioned regarding the shoulder and arm, except that if it is dislocated thus, it does not expose its owner to lameness unless it becomes dislocated by itself without being accompanied by the leg, in which case its owner must always limp.
Know that the thigh regains its strength in fifty days or somewhat more or less, all according to varying constitutions and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteen.
On fractures of the patella.
The patella is rarely fractured but often dislocated. If it is fractured, it may be either cracked or split into pieces, with or without accompanying wound.
Examine all this by touch, and reduce the fragments of the patella with your fingers until they meet and fit properly according to your ability to reduce, gentle manipulation, and skill. Then apply a bandage and splint if necessary.
Tie above the site of fracture, and then observe all conditions mentioned regarding other fractures such as heat and inflammation etc., treating each appropriately until healing is complete.
Chapter Sixteen.
On setting fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick called the tibia and the other thin called the fibula; they are subject to various types of fractures similar to those of the arm bones. Therefore, its treatment is like the treatment of an arm fracture, as the work is one. If all the bones are broken, the leg will be dislocated in all directions. If only the thinner bone is broken, the leg will be dislocated forward. And if only the thicker bone is broken from below, this is something that should not escape your notice.
Apply traction and alignment, and bind the fractures accordingly, unless in case of a severe fracture of the leg with many splinters, where traction should be lighter and gentler.
After aligning the fractures and completing your work on the leg, take two pieces of pine wood, which are used to flatten the spaces between the cracks in boards or can be made from date palm leaves.
Select two pieces that are slightly thick and not too thin, their length being equal to the length of the leg from the knee to the ankle. Then wrap each one with a cloth twice its length and place one on either side of the leg.
Let them extend from the knee to below the foot, then tie the two pieces at three points on both ends and in the middle. With this binding, the leg will be prevented from swaying left or right and will remain steadfastly fixed. A measuring rod made of wood along the length of the leg may also be used.
Place the splint to keep it still, especially if there is an accompanying wound. Then check the leg every day and pay careful attention to any swelling or inflammation, or other complications. When such things occur, treat them appropriately until recovery. The bone of the leg is usually set in about thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen
On fractures of the bones of the leg and toes
As for the heel, it never fractures. However, bones of the leg may sometimes fracture, while the toes rarely do; they are more often dislocated.
If there is a fracture in the bones of the leg and you see that some of them are protruding towards each other, have the patient stand with their foot flat on the ground like walking. Then step on what has risen above the fractured bones and press down to align them.
Until it returns to its place, then take the bandage and splint from above and place them beneath the foot on a small board with two flattened ends. Then bind the foot firmly with it after wrapping it with cloth and whatever else is needed.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage. If you find that bones which were broken or dislocated are now aligned, proceed as follows: if some of the fingers are fractured, straighten and align them according to the method I described for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the fractured finger along its length, slightly wider than it. Then place this board under the foot and bind it firmly if two or three or more bones are broken.
Make a splint for each finger according to its size, sloping and wrapped in soft cloth. Bind the board under the foot, ensuring that each of its ends rests outside the sole of the foot for proper alignment.
And remember to apply all that has been mentioned regarding signs and their corresponding treatments in other fractures as well.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the fracture of the woman's pelvis and pubis bone, and that of the man.
When the woman's pelvis is fractured, make her sit up and lean her back gently towards a pillow. Then have a midwife pack cotton into her vagina until it is full and forms a ball inside.
Then shake the woman gently and lift her hips slightly. The cotton will come out until it is at the vaginal opening like a ball, and the bone will be reduced. Then place a support on her back. Whenever she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can.
And return it to its original position, then resume the support for seven days or so until it heals. If you wish, you can use a sheep's bladder: attach a tube made of hollow reed to its mouth and insert the entire bladder into the vagina.
Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder inflates within the vagina. If the fracture is recent, fill it with cotton and apply what we have described until it heals. As for when the bone of the pubis in a man or woman breaks, this is how it is treated.
And treat it as we described for fractures of the hip bone. You are not unaware of the correctness in these rare and unusual fractures, especially if you have some experience and understand this book. Indeed, the fracture itself indicates what should be done in most cases.
If you follow the correct path in treating and reducing it, you will understand. As for when a man breaks his thigh bone near the hip joint, insert the penis into the fracture site, then place a bandage of cloth over it, secure it, and leave it alone for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen
On reduction of bone fractures accompanied by wounds
If someone suffers a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if it is a large bone such as the thighbone or armbone, he should hurry to separate himself from it at once, provided that the conditions for bloodletting are present, as mentioned earlier.
If the wound is bleeding profusely, it is necessary to immediately cauterize it with powdered vitriol if nothing else is available. Then proceed with reducing the fracture on that same day without delay.
If no inflammation occurs, wait until the ninth day to reduce the fracture. But if inflammation does occur, leave reduction until it subsides and do not approach the patient on the third or fourth day, for he may then exhibit bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding through the skin, gently push it back and align it using your hands with slight traction. If you are unable to do so manually, use this apparatus made of iron, which should be about seven or eight fingers long. Adjust its size according to the wound's measurement.
Therefore, the physician should prepare three or four of these apparatuses in various sizes according to his needs for treating different types of fractures. They should be made slightly thick so they do not bend during use.
The tip is sharp with a bend at its end; it extends upwards towards the thick part and below is very slender.
This instrument is called 'perem' in Greek, meaning a small lever. It should not be used with its sharp end pointing towards the protruding bone tip, and one should press it gently until the bone returns to some extent of straightness so that the edges of the fracture align with each other.
If its broken end is thin and the instrument did not grasp it properly, then do not cut off that bone tip until the instrument can take hold of it. If you are still unable to reduce the bone as described earlier, then cut it with a similar instrument to those mentioned.
Or, if possible, use one of the saws to cut it. Then clean up any remaining roughness and splinters on the bone. If you find that after reducing the bone there is severe pain, then know that the bone has not been properly reduced.
If you are able to properly reduce the bone, then do so as this will greatly benefit the patient. Once the bone has been set, dip a bandage in astringent black syrup and apply it, especially if it is summer. Do not use any greasy substance on the wound.
To prevent it from becoming foul and decaying, then apply plaster casts immediately after reducing the bone. Leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandage that is equal to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to tighten the wound with the fracture, as many ignorant doctors have done this causing either death or lame limbs for their patients. Therefore, let your bandage be loose and relaxed unlike the tightening used in other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some of the bad symptoms that were described, or if there is persistent pain in the area, then do not apply plaster casts. Instead, make bandages from strong cloth and bind it tightly with them.
If after a day or two you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, then remove the bandage soaked in syrup. Then apply lint and ointments such as the quadruple ointment, etc., as is customary for treating wounds. You should also loosen the bandage.
Inspect the wound every morning and evening until it heals and clears up. It is advisable to elevate the limb so that pus drains downward easily. If many days pass and the wound does not adhere or stop draining, know that there are small bone fragments remaining.
It is necessary to search the wound with forceps, and if any of those fragments are unhealed, remove them and extract them. If any remain unhealed and are hurting the limb, cut them and remove them in whatever way you can.
If there is swelling, inflammation, or any other type of corruption and foulness in the wound, it is necessary to treat each case with its corresponding treatment as described in its chapter.
And know that if a large bone is broken and exposed to the limb, such as the thighbone or armbone and other major limbs, do not attempt to extract it. For often death results from attempting this, so leave it alone until it rots, for perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you find a place suitable for treatment; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On treating complications that arise after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially those near joints, distorting the shape of the limb and perhaps preventing it from functioning normally.
If the complication is fresh, use astringent medications such as tragacanth, myrrh, aloe, and gum arabic, among others. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent liquid, egg white, or vinegar.
And apply this mixture to the complication with pressure, tying it securely and leaving it untied for several days before removing it and repeating the process until the complication subsides, if Allah wills. Or you may secure a copper plate tightly over it.
For copper has a property that removes all that is exposed in the limbs. If the complication has become hard and severe, and it becomes necessary to remove it, cut off its upper part or scrape away the excess flesh using forceps until it disappears. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One
In treating fractures, if the limb becomes thin after healing, and remains so abnormally afterwards
If a bone fracture becomes thinner and weaker after healing, it may be due to many reasons. One of these reasons might be either excessive weakening and improper tightening of ligaments, or overly tightening them to the point that nutrients cannot reach the limb, or excessive trembling, or movements at inappropriate times, or a lack of blood in the patient's body and weakness.
And its treatment is to nourish the patient and enrich his body until blood increases in him, and to use baths and bring happiness upon him, then apply warm wax on the limb to draw much nutrition towards it or to reduce trembling with lukewarm water. Until nutrients are increased therein and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two
In treating crookedly healed broken bones that have lost their proper function
When a limb has healed crookedly or with a twist after a bone fracture, causing deformity and ugliness in the appearance of that limb But if the limb has not lost its natural function, then it is not permissible to accept the claim of those who say that the bone should be broken again. Many ignorant physicians and bonesetters in our land do this.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great harm, but if the deformity and complexity are recent, then it is permissible to reduce them using water in which softening herbs such as papyrus leaves, comfrey roots, and marsh mallow have been boiled.
And it should be bandaged with softening dressings such as well-prepared diacanthum, or papyrus sap mixed with chicken fat and sheep suet, or dates mixed with pigeon dung, and other dissolving remedies.
And if the complexity persists despite this, then gentle and continuous massage should be employed using hands, along with movements of the limb in all directions at all times. But if the deformity has become firm and hardened and its treatment requires iron.
It is necessary to incise above and release the bone's attachment, cut what remains of the bone or complication with gentle cuts, employing gentleness in this with effort and care. Then treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three
When one of the jaws dislocates, it causes pain and severe discomfort to the patient. Therefore, it is imperative to reduce it immediately.
Do not delay it at all, for if delayed, the area will swell and reduction of the jaw becomes difficult. Therefore, do not move or extend it while it is swollen as this often causes harmful sprains and pain to the patient.
However, if it occurs, proceed with bloodletting the patient first, then allow the swelling to subside a little. Then manipulate the joint with warm water and oil, gently reduce it, and treat each part according to what is mentioned in its place.
And the sections on the jaws are arranged similarly from the top of the body downwards as discussed under fractures.
Chapter Twenty-Four
On treating the lower jawbone.
Rarely do the jaws dislocate except in few cases, and when they do, it happens in one of two ways: either they slip out of their places slightly and remain relatively relaxed, or they completely dislocate and become fully relaxed. If they dislocate to the extent of the chest, saliva flows from the patient's mouth, and he is unable to keep it in or clench his teeth together. If he speaks, his tongue stumbles. But if it is a slight dislocation, he can often reduce it himself with ease.
But if the dislocation is complete, do not rush to reduce it quickly and do not delay it at all. If the dislocation is on one side only, a servant should hold the patient's head while the physician inserts the index finger of one hand into the origin of the jaw inside the mouth.
Or, if the dislocation is on both sides, the physician should insert both thumbs into the mouth while using the other fingers from outside to align it. Then he should instruct the patient to relax his jaw and move it towards each side while gently guiding the jaw back into its place.
If it is difficult to reduce (the jaw), especially if all the joints are affected, then use medicament with hot water and oil until they can be easily reduced. Do not delay their reduction at all, as previously mentioned. When they have returned to their normal position and the patient's mouth has closed without being relaxed, then place wicks soaked in a preparation made from wax, oil, and rosewater on them.
Then tie it gently with a relaxed bandage, ensuring the patient lies on their back with their head elevated between two pillows to prevent movement neither to the right nor left and to avoid chewing solid food; instead, they should consume soft, liquid nourishment.
Until the pain has subsided and the jaw is healed, let him eat whatever he desires. He should open his mouth gently for eating, drinking, and yawning to ensure that the jaw remains healed.
If the jaw is difficult to reduce and has dislocated at some point without returning to its usual position, then constant feverishness, headaches, abdominal pain, or repeated vomiting may occur. If you observe these symptoms, know that it is severe (i.e., the dislocation is serious).
And many die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
On reducing a dislocated jaw and shoulder blade
As for the collarbone, it does not dislocate inwardly due to its connection with the chest. However, it can dislocate outwardly and this is noticeable by touch. To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back with their arms extended.
Then press the dislocation with your hand firmly until it returns into place. Then apply a bandage and wicks, and secure them. The end of the collarbone near the shoulder blade rarely dislocates; if it does, do not attempt to reduce it as described above.
Then apply a bandage, wicks, and secure them. Order the patient to remain quiet until healed. The same treatment is used to reduce a dislocated shoulder blade.
Chapter Twenty-Six
On dislocation of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder blade dislocates in three ways: one towards the armpit downwards, another towards the chest, and sometimes it dislocates upwards beyond the shoulder blade, which happens rarely.
It does not dislocate backwards behind the shoulder blade nor forwards near the nerve, and it commonly dislocates and exits downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh as it comes out quickly and enters swiftly.
As for those with much flesh, it is different; it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty. Sometimes a person may receive a blow or fall and the shoulder blade swells and becomes hot, leading one to believe that it has dislocated; therefore, it should not be examined until its condition is ascertained.
When this occurs, proceed as follows to treat it: if the dislocation is downwards towards the armpit, bring the dislocated shoulder blade close to the healthy one; you will find a clear difference between them, and you will feel that the head of the shoulder blade is rough. Upon touching under the armpit, you will find the head of the shoulder blade like an egg.
The patient cannot raise their hand to their ear nor move it in all its movements. Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, this will be apparent upon examination and not hidden.
This dislocation can be easily reduced if the shoulder blade is soft or if the patient is a child: have an attendant raise their hand upwards, then place your thumbs under the armpit and lift the dislocated shoulder blade upwards with force until it returns to its place while the attendant raises their hand and extends it upwards.
Then lower the hand downwards; if it returns quickly, if not, and the dislocation has existed for many days, then the patient should not bathe in hot water but should use ointments that relax and soften, such as boiling and mixing the roots of senna and linseed with water.
Then the patient should lie on their back, and a wool ball of moderate firmness should be placed under their armpit. The physician then places their elbow on the ball and holds the patient's hand with theirs, extending it downwards.
He pushes the ball with his foot; it returns to its place. If you wish, return it in this manner: prepare a man taller than the patient, position him at the side of the bed, insert his elbow underneath the armpit of the patient and raise it upwards so that the patient hangs in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards his abdomen. If the patient is light, do not attach anything else to him lest he be weighed down; otherwise, the fracture will return instantly. It may also be reset in another manner.
And that is, to place on the ground a long plank with a rounded end like the mouth of a mortar, not too thick nor too thin. Then place it under the armpit of the patient after placing a clean cloth on its head while the patient stands along the length of the plank.
Then he extends his hand downwards on the other side and also stretches his body strongly from that side, whereby the joint returns to its place quickly. But if it is difficult to reset using all methods mentioned above, then use this treatment: take a plank about two cubits long. And its width should be about four fingers and thickness like two fingers, with a rounded end to facilitate insertion into the depth of the armpit in this manner:
Then tie a soft cloth on the rounded head of the plank so that it does not hurt the patient. Then place it under the armpit, extend the hand and arm completely downwards along the plank and bind the plank to the forearm and wrist.
Then place the arm on a crossbeam, raise the shoulder and extend the hand downwards while allowing the rest of the body to hang from the other side. When the joint enters completely into its position, then place a moderate-sized ball made of wool under the armpit.
Then bring the prepared plaster made of flour, incense and egg white to the entire shoulder blade as it rotates from above. Then press the ball from under the armpit firmly, rotate the bandage around the plaster from above and tie his hand to his neck.
He should leave his hand unmoved for seven days, and it is necessary that he make the patient's food light until the member strengthens, as this is quicker for its recovery. Then after seven or five days, if it remains steady and has not weakened, movement may be started; if it holds firm without relaxing, then it will have healed.
But if the joint becomes dislocated repeatedly due to wetness or another cause, it is necessary to use cauterization with three sticks of incense as mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days but the joint does not remain stable.
If the bandage and tying are repeated several times but it does not remain firm, falls down, and cannot be raised up again, know that its tendon at the top of the elbow has either snapped, stretched, or weakened. Know then that the joint will never remain in place.
As for the dislocation that occurs towards the chest and breast, extending to the back, it should be reduced by pushing and pulling with both hands until it returns. All other methods of traction and treatment may be employed until it heals, but if there is stiffness in the member after healing and slowness in movement.
The patient should use the bath frequently so that the stiffness softens and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Seventh.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns to its place with difficulty. It can dislocate in all directions, especially towards the front or back, and its dislocation is obvious both to sight and touch regardless of how it has occurred.
When you compare the dislocated elbow with a normal one, it is clear that the joint appears hollow and the arm cannot be bent or brought to touch the chest. It is necessary to promptly reduce the dislocation before heat and inflammation set in.
If heat and inflammation have already set in, reduction will be difficult and the dislocation may not heal completely, especially if it has occurred towards the back. This is the worst type of dislocation and causes severe pain, often leading to death.
And if it is something that can be reduced, let a servant extend his hand with both hands and arms stretched out, and the doctor's hand from above the elbow and below it, pushing the joint back into place with the thumb of both hands or the base of the hand until it returns to its proper position.
And if the jaw is dislocated forward, it can be reduced by bending the hand repeatedly until striking with the base of the hand against the shoulder next to it. If the jaw does not return to its position, then use great force by having a servant pull on the arm.
And the patient should be held by a servant so that he does not move during traction. Then, the arm is wrapped in a folded cloth or wide bandage and moved to each side after the joint has been reduced with oiled hands for ease of manipulation.
Then, the joint is pushed back forcefully until it returns. After it has returned, a bandage with dough and egg white should be applied to hold it in place tightly. The arm should then be suspended from the patient's neck and left untouched.
If the joint stays in its place, then untie the bandage and leave it. But if the joint does not seem secure, prepare a new bandage and tie it tightly until it becomes firm, then untie it. If there is swelling after reducing the joint, do not tie the bandage.
If there is difficulty in movement, use gentle massage and compression with warm compresses until it becomes soft. Or, place a poultice of fat on the joint, tie it on, and leave it for a day and night before removing it and entering the bath. If he sweats, rub the joint moderately.
Then, apply the poultice again once or twice more with repeated bathing until it becomes soft. If you wish to use a moistened cow's foot with butter and grease applied, do so repeatedly to make it soften and return to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of wrist dislocation.
The wrist often dislocates and returns, easier than other joints, except that it should be quickly reset at the moment it dislocates to prevent swelling or inflammation.
Place the wrist of the affected arm on a board and extend the patient's hand. The physician should place their palm on the joint and push until it reduces, but they should first ensure that the dislocation has moved back into its proper position.
If the dislocation is prominent on the outer side of the hand, place the inner aspect of the hand on the board so that the physician's palm can reach the joint. If it reduces immediately, apply a cold compress to prevent swelling.
Leave it alone; do not attempt to reduce it again if it does not immediately reduce, as the joint will remain dislocated but cause no harm to the patient unless the hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve has been severed or crushed, and there is no treatment for it except to apply pressure with a bandage, which may help or not. After reducing the dislocation, apply the compress we described, then tie it tightly and leave it on for five days.
Then remove the bandage and exercise the hand. If its movement is impaired or there is any stiffness, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it becomes limber.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Treatment of Finger Dislocations.
When a finger dislocates towards any direction, if it dislocates outwards or inwards, extend the finger and push the dislocation back into place with your thumb, then bind the finger's head and suspend it towards the direction it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it aligns properly. Do this daily. If it is bound at night according to the same description, release it during the day, exercise it, and rebind it at night until it regains its strength.
Similarly, if it dislocates inwards towards the palm, bind it accordingly and follow the same procedure until it heals. Do likewise for dislocations in other directions.
Chapter Thirty
In treating locked jaw.
If the jaw becomes dislocated from one of the vertebrae or many of them shift from their places, there is no treatment for it because death will quickly occur to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's bowels will move without control, and they will not be able to hold them back. Often, some of their limbs, either both legs or arms, or one of them, become paralyzed. But if only one vertebra has shifted from its place, it often goes back into position.
When the dislocation is in any of the four directions, that which dislocates backwards is called a hump. Its treatment is as follows: if the hump has occurred due to old age, there is no treatment for it and it will not recover from it at all. But if it has happened due to a fall or blow or similar cause...
I have mentioned these in previous writings, but I will summarize them here as most of what has been written before is of little benefit. I have clarified and illustrated the instruments more accurately than they were previously described and explained.
I say that the hump which occurs at the front of the chest, there is no treatment for it nor recovery from it. Similarly, those that occur on the sides are also not treatable. Only those that occur in the back can be treated, particularly as I will describe: lay the patient face down.
Place the patient on a flat platform near a wall, with a wet cloth underneath to prevent discomfort to their chest. Then insert a vertical wooden post dug into a hole in the ground at one end of the platform near the patient's head, and another at the opposite end near their feet.
Have a servant hold onto the post near the patient's head, ensuring it is not firmly fixed in the hole. Have another servant hold onto the post at the opposite end. Then wrap a soft, close-fitting cloth around the patient's chest and under their armpits, extending one end of the cloth to the post near their head and tie it there.
Then tie another cloth tightly above the patient's hips, knees, and ankles. Gather all the bindings together and tie them to the post at the patient's feet. Then each servant should pull on their respective posts while ensuring that neither post leaves its dug-in position.
However, these two methods are unproven as we have said. The physician places his hands on the node with force until it returns to its original position or places a board on it and then leans on the board with both knees until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, he takes a board about three arms in length.
He digs into the wall that we have said should be near the patient a place to insert one end of the board, then places the middle of the board on the prominence, and the physician puts his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until the nodes creak.
And it returns to its place. If you wish, you may use a screw that is turned by hand; this involves inserting two stakes into the ground at the head of the patient's bed, each about an arm in length, and two more stakes at his feet.
Let there be about a span between each stake, and make a hole in each stake for the screw to pass through. Secure the stakes firmly in the ground so that they do not move at all. Then insert a wooden dowel, which is the screw that will be used to twist the band.
At one end of the screw, secure a wooden dowel about a span long that can be twisted. Do likewise for the other two stakes. Then attach the band that you have tied at the patient's chest to the screw at his head and the bands you have tied on his legs to the screws at his feet.
Then, at each screw, a servant stands and turns the handle that rotates the screw. The physician straightens the prominence as we have described. Here is an illustration of the screw, the bed, and the patient:
After the prominence returns and the position is straightened, you should then apply a dressing made of gauze soaked in egg white. Then apply a compress, followed by a bandage made from a board that is three fingers wide or more, and its length should be equal to the size of the prominence.
Then bind it with a bandage that should be used, and let the patient consume soft foods until he recovers. If there is still some hardness remaining at the site after recovery, then you should use ointments that relax and soften along with the use of the board we have described for an extended period.
A lead plate may be used for this purpose, but it is not recommended to use it at the end of the spinal cord as it might cause a fracture which could be an extraneous bone growth. Do not attempt this treatment lest death should occur.
The Thirty-First Chapter.
In treating a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip and shoulder joints are only subject to dislocation, unlike other joints which can also suffer from slight displacement or subluxation. The hip joint may dislocate in four ways.
This dislocation occurs inwardly, outwardly, forwardly or backwardly. The most common is inward dislocation, while forward and backward dislocations are less frequent. A sign of inward dislocation is that the healthy leg appears longer when compared to the affected leg.
The knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their leg at the hip. The area around the buttock will feel warm and swollen due to the dislocation of the femoral head.
The signs of outward dislocation are opposite to those of inward dislocation. For forward dislocation, the patient can extend their leg completely but cannot bend it without pain at the knee, making walking difficult.
For backward dislocation, urine and feces are retained, the buttock is tender, and the patient walks on their heel. The signs of backward dislocation include an inability to extend or bend the knee before bending the hip, with the affected leg appearing shorter than the other.
The hip is relaxed, and the femoral head is located at the sciatic notch. If a dislocation has occurred in the past without attempted reduction and remains untreated, there is no cure for it.
If an old dislocation is discovered, it should not be attempted to reduce. However, if a recent dislocation of any kind has occurred, the joint should be gently manipulated and moved inwardly, outwardly, leftward and rightward until reduction is achieved.
If reduction is not achieved through gentle manipulation, then a strong assistant should be summoned to extend the leg from below using both hands or a bandage tied above the knee. Another assistant should help by pushing from above.
That he should enter his hands under his armpits, then pull a soft bandage over the thigh root and have another servant hold its end; it may be stretched either from front towards the chest or from behind towards the back.
And all of them are stretched together in one motion until the patient's body is raised from the ground and remains suspended. This type of stretching is common to all four kinds, but if the jaw does not return as mentioned earlier, each kind requires its own specific treatment.
As for its specific treatment, if the jaw is turned inward, the patient should lie on his healthy side, and a bandage should be tied over the thigh root between the hip head and the area under the armpit.
Then the bandage is stretched upwards from under the armpit towards the chest, and another strong servant aids by holding with his arms the thick part of the sick thigh and pulls it out strongly until it returns to its place.
This method is easier than other treatments used to reset this limb. If it proves difficult and you are unable to attempt this type of treatment, then tie the patient's legs together with a strong, soft bandage over the heels and knees.
The bandage should be about four fingers wide, and the sick leg should be extended two fingers more than the other. Then suspend the patient from a beam in the house, with his feet about two cubits above the ground.
Then order a strong servant to hold the hip head and another servant to suspend the patient. Push the servant holding the hip with force, and the joint will quickly return to its place. As for its specific treatment if the jaw is turned outward...
The patient should lie on the couch as described earlier for those with a hump. Tie the bandage around his sick leg and chest, then place one beam under his legs and another under his head. Place an additional beam securely in the middle of the couch.
Wet cloths should be wrapped around it so that they do not harm the patient. The beam should be placed between his thighs to prevent him from slipping down during stretching. Then each servant pulls in his direction, while the doctor uses his hands to align the hip joint if it returns.
Otherwise, place the board upon it and press firmly as mentioned in the arch, until the patient is lying on his sound side. If the dislocation is towards the front, then extend the patient's leg slightly while remaining in this position on the couch.
The physician places his right hand upon the injured hip, then presses it with the other hand while maintaining continuous pressure downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is towards the back, the patient should not be extended downwards.
He should be elevated off the ground, rather he should be placed on something solid. Likewise, his dislocated hip should be external rotated according to the description of his position on the couch while facing downwards with the straps tightened.
As previously mentioned, pressure with the board should also be applied to the point where the joint has protruded. Once the hip joint has been properly reduced and its sign is evident (extension of the leg),
If you observe that both hips are level and the patient can bend and straighten their leg without difficulty, know that the joint has been properly reduced. Then bind the thighs together and apply a bandage, tying it firmly to prevent any movement of the hip in either direction.
The patient should remain at rest for three to four days, then the bandage and splint should be removed and the leg should be gently moved. If both hips appear equal in height, then know that the dislocation has been fixed and the patient may begin walking. However, if there is any laxity,
then return and bind it again as before, leaving it on for three more days. Then remove it and allow the patient to walk slowly until they regain their strength, God willing.
Chapter Two and Thirty.
On treating dislocation of the knee joint.
The kneecap dislocates in three directions: inwards, outwards, and downwards (towards the back), but it does not dislocate forwards. The sign of dislocation is that the patient is unable to bring their thigh towards their knee.
If the wound does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. Then, make the patient sit with his legs extended if he has the strength to do so, and have a servant sit behind him to hold his waist and gently pull him backwards. Then, sit on the patient's thighs.
And press your back against his face, place his leg between yours, then grasp his knee with both hands and interlock your fingers on it. Then squeeze the sides of his knee firmly with your hands while another servant presses his foot until the kneecap returns to its place.
The sign that it has returned is when the leg adheres to the thigh smoothly and without resistance. Then, dress it and bind the leg to the thigh with a bandage for three or four days, after which release them. Do not allow him to walk much until he regains his strength.
However, if you are unable to return it using the method described above, then apply strong traction using the apparatus mentioned earlier for dislocated hips until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In treating an ankle fracture.
The heel may simply slip out of place or be completely dislocated, with the fracture occurring either internally or externally. The sign of dislocation is that the heel appears swollen and prominent on the side towards which it has shifted.
However, if it has slipped out of place, it is easy to reduce by gently extending the foot and pushing until it returns. But if it has been completely dislocated, the patient should sit with his legs extended while a strong servant holds him firmly from behind at the waist.
Then, grasp the foot with your right hand at its upper part and with your left hand at its lower part near the ankle. Pull the foot towards you with your right hand twice, then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot gently towards the leg with your right hand.
Repeat this process twice as described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg for the third time while simultaneously pulling on the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts and the foot appears straight, do not repeat the procedure.
If its return (the flow of urine) is prevented as we have described, then lay the patient on their back on the ground. Secure a peg and drive it firmly into the ground so that it falls between their thighs. Wrap the patient in cloth to prevent them from being harmed, then adjust the servant's thigh.
Then another servant extends the patient's leg, either by hand or using a rope tied around the patient's neck. Then each servant pulls while the other holds firm, ensuring that the patient's body does not slip downwards. The physician then straightens the joint with his hands.
And another servant holds the healthy leg down so that the joint returns quickly. Once the joint has returned and its soundness is confirmed, then lift the splint and strap it tightly using bindings. Tie the foot downwards, ensuring to avoid the nerve located above the heel from behind.
So that the binding is not too tight and harms him. Then leave it for two or three days; if the binding has loosened, then tighten it again on the third or fourth day, and do not allow the patient to walk for forty days.
For if the patient tries to walk before this period, it is not certain that the joint will not dislocate and become ruined, and it will not accept treatment afterwards. If he develops an inflammatory swelling, then do not use any of the remedies we have mentioned for its treatment or reduction except in other cases.
Chapter Forty-three.
In treating the dislocation of a toe joint.
It is necessary to gently straighten what has been dislocated without force, as this is not difficult but easy. If the joint is in some toes of the back of the foot, then place the patient's foot on a level spot on the ground.
Or place it on a board while standing as if walking, then step on the protruding part of that joint with your foot with force until it returns and becomes straight, without any prominence remaining.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot, with two raised ends that support the entire foot. Secure it tightly using bandages for three days, then remove and immobilize him from walking for many days until it becomes strong and its return is secure, if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Five
In types of the jaw that occurs with wound or with fracture or with both together.
When such a thing happens and its treatment is delayed, death often ensues.
Therefore, one should only undertake treating such cases if they are skilled and experienced, compassionate, careful, not hasty nor reckless. Initially, they should use remedies that alleviate hot swellings only, and then leave the patient alone.
O Allah, grant healing only if You will for good, with ease in sickness and hope of recovery. So relieve him from the beginning before the hot swelling appears.
If the bone returns to its place, use remedies that alleviate hot swellings and treat the wound with suitable dry ointments. If the jaw is fractured with splinters protruding, remove them. And follow what was mentioned earlier regarding simple fractures in their respective places.
Strive diligently and keep yourself away from the path of error, as I have previously advised you. This is safer for your reputation and better for your practice, if Allah wills.
